The Majority Ash'ari View: The Name Is Identical to the Named
Sanusi states the Ash'ari view and provides three Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the divine name is identical to the named essence and how they support it with Quranic verses and respond to objections.

Core Idea
For the majority of Ash'aris, the name and the named are one reality.

This lesson introduces the predominant Ash'ari view on the relationship between Allah's names and His essence. The author presents the position that the name is the same as the named, supporting it with three Quranic proofs. We will also see how they address an apparent objection raised by later commentators.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Notice how Sanusi states his position, then immediately brings Quranic evidence.
The passage is dense with citations.
2
Identify the three Quranic proofs
Find the three verses Sanusi uses. The first two are about glorifying the name and worshipping only names. The third is from a line of poetry.
The third proof is a line by Labid al-Amiri.
3
Understand al-Sa'd's objection
Al-Sa'd notes that using the verses as evidence implies a distinction between the name and the essence, because glorification and worship are offered to the essence, not the name. How does Sanusi respond?
4
Learn Sanusi's response
Sanusi clarifies that glorification can apply to the name itself, meaning the essence is beyond naming. He also explains the verse about the deities as names without named entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أكثر الأشاعرة الاسم عين المسمى. قال تعالى: {سبح اسم ربك الأعلى}. {وما تعبدون من دونه إلا أسماء}. {إلى الحول ثم اسم السلام عليكما} يعني السلام نفسه. قال السعد في شرح مقاصده وفي الاستدلال بالآيتين اعتراف بالمغايرة حيث يقال التسبيح والعبادة للذات دون الأسماء. انتهى. على أن التسبيح يصح لنفس الاسم بمعنى تنزيه الذات عن التسمية كما في البيضاوي. والعبادة تعلق به ظاهر الفرض إشارة إلى أن هذه الألهة عدم في حضرة الألوهية فكأنها أسماء لا مسميات لها. ولفظ اسم في البيت مهم إشارة إلى أنه ليس سلاما حقيقيا إذ هما لا يأمنان بعده. والبيت للبيد العامري: فقوما وقولا بالذي تعلمان ... إلى الحول ثم اسم السلام عليكما. وقبل: الاسم غير المسمى لقوله تعالى: {وله الأسماء الحسنى} ولابد من المغايرة بين الشيء وما حواله. ولقد ورد الأسماء مع اتحاد المسمى ولو كان عينه لزم أن من قال نار إلى غير ذلك من المفاسد. والتحقيق: أنه إن أريد من الاسم اللفظ فهو غير مسماه قطعا، وإن أريد به ما يريم منه فهو عين المسمى. ولا فرق في ذلك بين جامد ومشتق.
Most Ash'aris said: The name is identical to the named. He, exalted, said: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High.' 'And what you worship besides Him are only names.' 'After a year then the name of peace upon you both' meaning peace itself. Al-Sa'd said in his commentary on his Aims: 'In using the two verses as evidence there is an admission of difference where it is said that glorification and worship are for the essence, not the names.' End. However, glorification can apply to the name itself in the sense of declaring the essence free from naming, as in al-Baydawi. And worship is attached to it in apparent assumption as an indication that these deities are non-existent in the presence of divinity, so they are as if names without named entities. The word 'name' in the verse is significant as an indication that it is not real peace, since they would not be safe after a year. The verse is by al-Ba'id al-'Amiri: 'So stand and say what you know ... after a year then the name of peace upon you both.' And it was said: The name is other than the named because of His saying: 'To Him belong the most beautiful names' and there must be difference between a thing and what is around it. And indeed names are mentioned while the named is one; if it were identical, it would entail that whoever said 'fire' etc. — such corruptions. The verified position: if by 'name' is meant the word, then it is certainly other than its named; and if by it is meant what designates it, then it is identical to the named. There is no difference in this between a derived and a non-derived term.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the theological position regarding the relationship between the divine name (الاسم) and the named (المسمى) essence. It begins by stating the predominant Ash'ari view: the name is the named itself. The author supports this with Quranic verses: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and 'You worship only names.' In the second verse, the word 'name' is argued to be a reference to the essence. The commentary by al-Sa'd is then cited, who notes that using these verses as proof actually implies a distinction (مغايرة) between the essence and the names, because worship is directed to the essence, not the names. The author clarifies that glorification can apply to the name itself, meaning the essence is beyond any name. The verses referring to 'the name of peace' after a year indicate that the name is not the same as the reality of peace. Then the opposite view is presented: the name is other than the named, citing 'To Him belong the most beautiful names' — which requires a distinction between the thing and its attributes. If the name were identical to the named, absurd consequences follow, such as equating the word 'fire' with fire itself. The author then provides the reconciliation (التحقيق): if by 'name' is meant the linguistic expression, it is clearly different from the named; if by it is meant the referent or meaning, then it is identical. This applies to both derived and non-derived terms. The passage thus presents both sides and resolves the debate by distinguishing two senses of 'name'.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
المغايرة
العينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the divine names are entirely separate entities from Allah's essence.
Correction: The majority Ash'ari view affirms that the name is identical to the named, meaning the name points to the essence without separation.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Ash'aris, what is the relationship between al-ism (the name) and al-musamma (the named)?
A) The name is entirely different from the named.
B) The name is identical to the named.
C) The name is a part of the named.
D) The name is a created attribute of the named.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari majority holds that Allah's name and His essence are identical, supported by Quranic proofs and clarified against objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' or recite Allah's names, do you feel you are directly connecting to His essence, or are the names just labels? How would your reverence change if you truly believed the name and the named are one?
?
The lesson shows that glorifying the name is glorifying the essence itself. In your daily prayers and dhikr, do you glorify Allah's names as if you are standing before His very being, or do you sometimes let the words become empty? What would it take to make them real for you?
?
The objection raised by al-Sa'd reminds us that even sincere believers can misunderstand the relationship between Allah's names and His essence. Have you ever treated a divine name casually, as if it were separate from the reality it points to? What can you change today to ensure your worship is directed to the essence, not just a word?

The Reconciliation: Two Meanings of 'Name'
Sanusi presents the opposing view and the final resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opposing view that the name is other than the named and the final reconciliation distinguishing the linguistic expression from the meaning.

Core Idea
The key to resolving the debate is to distinguish between the word 'name' and the intended meaning.

Having presented the mainstream Ash'ari position, the author now addresses the view that the name is different from the named. He shows why this view seems plausible and then offers a precise distinction that resolves the apparent conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage to understand the opposing argument and the resolution.
Note the use of the verse 'To Him belong the most beautiful names'.
2
Identify the opposing argument
The opposing view cites Quranic verses requiring a distinction between the thing and its attributes. Find the proof and the reasoning.
The argument: there must be difference between a thing and what is around it.
3
Consider the absurd consequences
If the name were identical to the named, saying 'fire' would mean the actual fire, which leads to absurdities.
Sanusi uses this reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the final distinction
The verified position distinguishes two meanings of 'name': the word (لفظ) and the referent (ما يريم منه). The word is other than the named; the referent is identical.
This distinction applies to both derived and non-derived terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أكثر الأشاعرة الاسم عين المسمى. قال تعالى: {سبح اسم ربك الأعلى}. {وما تعبدون من دونه إلا أسماء}. {إلى الحول ثم اسم السلام عليكما} يعني السلام نفسه. قال السعد في شرح مقاصده وفي الاستدلال بالآيتين اعتراف بالمغايرة حيث يقال التسبيح والعبادة للذات دون الأسماء. انتهى. على أن التسبيح يصح لنفس الاسم بمعنى تنزيه الذات عن التسمية كما في البيضاوي. والعبادة تعلق به ظاهر الفرض إشارة إلى أن هذه الألهة عدم في حضرة الألوهية فكأنها أسماء لا مسميات لها. ولفظ اسم في البيت مهم إشارة إلى أنه ليس سلاما حقيقيا إذ هما لا يأمنان بعده. والبيت للبيد العامري: فقوما وقولا بالذي تعلمان ... إلى الحول ثم اسم السلام عليكما. وقبل: الاسم غير المسمى لقوله تعالى: {وله الأسماء الحسنى} ولابد من المغايرة بين الشيء وما حواله. ولقد ورد الأسماء مع اتحاد المسمى ولو كان عينه لزم أن من قال نار إلى غير ذلك من المفاسد. والتحقيق: أنه إن أريد من الاسم اللفظ فهو غير مسماه قطعا، وإن أريد به ما يريم منه فهو عين المسمى. ولا فرق في ذلك بين جامد ومشتق.
Most Ash'aris said: The name is identical to the named. He, exalted, said: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High.' 'And what you worship besides Him are only names.' 'After a year then the name of peace upon you both' meaning peace itself. Al-Sa'd said in his commentary on his Aims: 'In using the two verses as evidence there is an admission of difference where it is said that glorification and worship are for the essence, not the names.' End. However, glorification can apply to the name itself in the sense of declaring the essence free from naming, as in al-Baydawi. And worship is attached to it in apparent assumption as an indication that these deities are non-existent in the presence of divinity, so they are as if names without named entities. The word 'name' in the verse is significant as an indication that it is not real peace, since they would not be safe after a year. The verse is by al-Ba'id al-'Amiri: 'So stand and say what you know ... after a year then the name of peace upon you both.' And it was said: The name is other than the named because of His saying: 'To Him belong the most beautiful names' and there must be difference between a thing and what is around it. And indeed names are mentioned while the named is one; if it were identical, it would entail that whoever said 'fire' etc. — such corruptions. The verified position: if by 'name' is meant the word, then it is certainly other than its named; and if by it is meant what designates it, then it is identical to the named. There is no difference in this between a derived and a non-derived term.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the theological position regarding the relationship between the divine name (الاسم) and the named (المسمى) essence. It begins by stating the predominant Ash'ari view: the name is the named itself. The author supports this with Quranic verses: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and 'You worship only names.' The author then provides the reconciliation (التحقيق): if by 'name' is meant the linguistic expression, it is clearly different from the named; if by it is meant the referent or meaning, then it is identical. This applies to both derived and non-derived terms. The passage thus presents both sides and resolves the debate by distinguishing two senses of 'name'.

The Name Is Other Than the Named (Ash'ari Doctrine)
Author argues that the name is other than the named using Quran and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris distinguish between the name of Allah and His Essence, and how they use Quranic evidence and linguistic reasoning to argue that the name is other than the named.

Core Idea
The name indicates the named but is not identical to it.

In this passage, the author defends a key Ash'ari principle: the names of Allah are not the same as His Essence. This distinction is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphism and misunderstanding the nature of worship. The author provides Quranic verses and logical analysis to establish that the name is a sign, not the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument.
The Arabic text has been reconstructed due to OCR issues.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
Notice the two verses cited and how the author interprets them to support distinction.
3
Understand the linguistic examples
The author gives two types of difference (wording and meaning). See how they apply to names.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion that names are other than the named, but also that names are signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الاسم غير المسمى كما عليه أكثر الأشاعرة. قال تعالى: {سَبِّحِ اسْمَ رَبِّكَ} [الأعلى:1]، [؟]. وإيضاح ذلك: أن التسبيح إنما يتعلق بالاسم لفظاً، والمراد به الذات تقديساً وتعظيماً، وإلا لزم تعدد الذات. وقوله تعالى: {وَلِلَّهِ الْأَسْمَاءُ الْحُسْنَى} [الأعراف:180] يدل على المغايرة، لأن الإضافة تقتضي غيرية المضاف والمضاف إليه. فالأسماء غير المسمى، كما أن الصفة غير الموصوف. فلا يقال إن الاسم عين المسمى، بل هو دال عليه. وقد مثلوا بالتغاير في اللفظ كقولنا: زيد قائم، فإن اللفظين مختلفان، والتغاير في المعنى كقولنا: زيد كاتب، فالكاتب غير زيد باعتبار المعنى. فثبت أن الاسم غير المسمى.
Know that the name is other than the named, as is the position of most Ash'aris. He (Most High) said: {Glorify the name of your Lord} [Al-A'la 87:1], [؟]. And clarification of that: that glorification is attached to the name verbally, but the intended meaning is the Essence, sanctifying and magnifying, otherwise multiplicity of Essence would follow. And His statement (Most High): {And to Allah belong the Most Beautiful Names} [Al-A'raf 7:180] indicates difference, because the construct state requires otherness between the construct and the annexed. So the names are other than the named, just as the attribute is other than the described. So one does not say that the name is identical to the named, rather it indicates it. And they gave examples of difference in wording like our saying: Zayd is standing, for the two words are different, and difference in meaning like our saying: Zayd is a writer, for the writer is other than Zayd by way of meaning. So it is established that the name is other than the named.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the Ash'ari doctrine that the name (ism) is distinct from the named (musamma). The author begins by stating the position, then provides scriptural evidence from the Quranic verse 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (87:1) and 'To Allah belong the Most Beautiful Names' (7:180). He explains that in the first verse, the command to glorify is directed toward the name verbally, but the intended object of glorification is the Divine Essence itself; if the name were identical to the Essence, then glorifying the name would imply multiplicity of deities. The second verse is adduced because the possessive construction (asma'un Allah) implies that the names are other than the one to whom they belong. The author then gives linguistic analogies: difference can be in wording (as in 'Zayd is standing' where the two words are distinct) or in meaning (as in 'Zayd is a writer' where 'writer' is a different concept from 'Zayd'). The conclusion is that names are signs indicating the named, not identical to it.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
التسبيح
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the name itself is the object of worship, e.g., because we say 'Glorify the name of your Lord'.
Correction: Ash'aris clarify that the name is a means of indicating the Essence; glorification is ultimately directed to the Essence, not the name as a separate entity.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on the relationship between the name (ism) and the named (musamma)?
A) The name is identical to the named.
B) The name is other than the named.
C) The name is a part of the named.
D) The name is a creation of the named.

Key Takeaway
For Ash'aris, the names of Allah are not identical to His Essence; they are signs that point to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Bismillah', do you treat the phrase as a mere label, or do you consciously let it direct your heart to the reality of Allah? How can you train yourself to move from the name to the One named in every utterance?
?
If the names of Allah are signs that point to Him, then how carefully do you consider your own speech about Allah? Does this truth make you more cautious to avoid saying anything that misrepresents His Essence?
?
The verse commands you to 'Glorify the name of your Lord'. Are you content with glorifying with your tongue alone, or will you commit to glorifying through your actions and inner state, knowing that the name is a pathway to the One it indicates?

The Essential Attributes: Existence, Eternity, and Oneness
The poet enumerates the essential necessary attributes of Allah and negates any opposite, likeness, or partner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core attributes of Allah that are necessary by reason and scripture: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, oneness, and absolute transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah's oneness and transcendence are the foundation of Islamic theology.

The poet begins by listing the attributes that are wajib (necessary) for Allah. These include wujud (existence), qidam (pre-eternity), baqa' (everlastingness), qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence), wahdaniyya (oneness), and tanzih (transcendence) from any opposite, likeness, partner, parent, or child. This passage establishes the basic framework for understanding Allah's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first portion of the poem from 'فواجب' to 'والأصدقا'. Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the attributes listed
List the attributes mentioned: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, oneness, transcendence.
3
Understand the proof for pre-eternity
The poet says 'the proof of this is pre-eternity' — meaning the attribute of qidam itself is evidence for the impossibility of non-existence.
4
Reflect on transcendence
Note the items from which Allah is transcendent: opposite, likeness, partner, parent, child. These negate any possible similarity or association.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فواجب له الوجود والقدم كذا بقاء لا يشاب بالعدم وأنه لما ينال العدم مخالف برهان هذا القدم قيامه بالنفس وحدانية منزهة أوصافه سنية عن ضد أو شبه شريك مطلقا ووالد كذا الولد والأصدقا أمرا وعلم والرضا كما ثبت وقدرة إرادة وغايرت وعلمه ولا يقال مكتسب فاتبع سبيل الحق واطرح الربى حياته كذا الكلام السمع ثم البصر بذي أتانا السمع فهل له إدراك أو لا خلف وعند قوم صح فيه الوقف حي عليم قادر مريد سمع بصير ما يشا يريد
So wajib for Him is existence (al-wujud) and pre-eternity (al-qidam), likewise everlastingness (al-baqa') untainted by non-existence. And that He is opposed to what is subject to non-existence; the proof of this is pre-eternity. His self-subsistence (qiyamuhu bi al-nafs), oneness (wahdaniyya), His attributes are exalted and transcend (munazzaha) from opposite (didd), likeness (shibh), partner (sharik) absolutely, and parent (walid), likewise child (walad) and associates (asdiqa'). Command (amr), knowledge (ilm), and pleasure (rida) as established, and power (qudra), will (irada), and they are different. And His knowledge, it is not said to be acquired (muktasab). So follow the path of truth and abandon doubts. His life (hayat), likewise speech (kalam), hearing (sam'), then sight (basar); by this hearing has come to us. So does He have perception (idrak) or not? There is dispute, and according to some people, suspension (waqf) is correct in it. Living (hayy), Knowing (alim), Powerful (qadir), Willing (murid), Hearing (sami'), Seeing (basir), He wills whatever He wills.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the divine attributes. The poet lists the necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) for Allah: existence (wujud), pre-eternity (qidam), everlastingness (baqa'), self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs), oneness (wahdaniyya), and transcendence (tanzih) from any opposite, likeness, partner, parent, child, or associate. Then he mentions attributes like amr (command?), ilm (knowledge), rida (pleasure), qudra (power), irada (will) — noting that they are distinct (ghayarat). He emphasizes that Allah's knowledge is not acquired (muktasab). Then he mentions life (hayat), speech (kalam), hearing (sam'), sight (basar), stating that the revelation (sam') came with these. There is a dispute about whether Allah has idrak (perception) distinct from these; some suspend judgment (waqf). The passage concludes with a summary: Allah is Hayy, Alim, Qadir, Murid, Sami', Basir, and He wills whatever He wills.

Key Terms
واجب
وجود
قدم
بقاء
قيام بالنفس
وحدانية
تنزيه
ضد
شبه
شريك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the attributes listed as wajib for Allah in this passage?
A) الوجود
B) القدم
C) القدرة
D) الوحدانية

Key Takeaway
Allah must possess existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, oneness, and be absolutely transcendent from any form of comparison or partnership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's oneness (wahdaniyya), do you ever silently entertain the thought that something else shares His power, even slightly? What does this passage demand you uproot from your heart?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is absolutely transcendent with no likeness at all, how would that change the way you try to visualize Him in your prayers? Are you still comfortable with mental images you may hold?
?
The proof says pre-eternity prevents non-existence. How does this truth impact your fear of death or your attachment to this temporary world? Does your daily life reflect certainty that only Allah is everlasting?

The Divine Attributes: Knowledge, Will, and Their Distinct Nature
The poet lists attributes like command, knowledge, pleasure, power, will, emphasizing their distinctness and that Allah's knowledge is unacquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of command, knowledge, pleasure, power, and will are distinct from one another, and that His knowledge is not acquired.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are real and distinct; His knowledge is inherent, not learned.

After establishing the essential attributes of essence, the poet turns to attributes related to action and perception. He mentions amr (command?), ilm (knowledge), rida (pleasure), qudra (power), and irada (will), and emphasizes that these are different (ghayarat) from each other. He also stresses that Allah's knowledge is not acquired (muktasab), warning the student to follow the truth and abandon doubts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read from 'أمرا وعلم' to 'واطرح الربى'. Identify each attribute mentioned.
2
Understand the distinctness
Note the phrase 'وغايرت' indicating that these attributes are distinct in meaning.
3
Reflect on knowledge not acquired
Allah's knowledge is not gained from anything; it is inherent. This refutes those who claim otherwise.
4
Take the advice
The poet says 'So follow the path of truth and abandon doubts' — emphasizing certainty in these matters.

So wajib for Him is existence (al-wujud) and pre-eternity (al-qidam), likewise everlastingness (al-baqa') untainted by non-existence. And that He is opposed to what is subject to non-existence; the proof of this is pre-eternity. His self-subsistence (qiyamuhu bi al-nafs), oneness (wahdaniyya), His attributes are exalted and transcend (munazzaha) from opposite (didd), likeness (shibh), partner (sharik) absolutely, and parent (walid), likewise child (walad) and associates (asdiqa'). Command (amr), knowledge (ilm), and pleasure (rida) as established, and power (qudra), will (irada), and they are different. And His knowledge, it is not said to be acquired (muktasab). Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the divine attributes. The poet lists the necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) for Allah: existence (wujud), pre-eternity (qidam), everlastingness (baqa'), self-subsistence (qiyam bi al-nafs), oneness (wahdaniyya), and transcendence (tanzih) from any opposite, likeness, partner, parent, child, or associate. There is a dispute about whether Allah has idrak (perception) distinct from these; some suspend judgment (waqf). Negating Opposites, Partners, and Family from Allah
The poet enumerates six entities negated from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological necessity of negating any opposite, likeness, partner, parent, child, or companions from Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely unique—no opposite, no partner, no family.

This passage opens with a powerful negation: Allah is free from any opposite, likeness, or partner, and also from parent, child, or companions. These negations establish the purest form of monotheism (tawhid) and protect against anthropomorphism and polytheism. Let's explore each term and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic negation line
Read the Arabic phrase 'عن ضد أو شبه شريك مطلقا ووالد كذا الولد والأصدقا' aloud. Notice the rhythm and the key terms of negation.
The word 'مطلقا' emphasizes absolute negation.
2
Identify each negated entity
List the six things negated: ضد (opposite), شبه (likeness), شريك (partner), والد (parent), ولد (child), أصدقا (companions).
Opposite and likeness are related to philosophical objections; partner, parent, child, companions are scriptural and rational denials.
3
Understand why each negation matters
Ask yourself: Why must Allah have no opposite? Why no likeness? Why no partner? Why no family? Connect each to core Islamic doctrines like tawhid, transcendence, and the impossibility of divine attributes being shared.
Negating opposite removes any equal rival; negating likeness removes anthropomorphism; negating partner and family reject polytheism and Christian/sabian beliefs.
4
Reflect on the theological impact
This single line sets the foundation for all subsequent attribute affirmations. Without these negations, affirmations could mislead. Consider how these denials shape your understanding of Allah's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن ضد أو شبه شريك مطلقا ووالد كذا الولد والأصدقا. وقدرة إرادة وغايرت وعلمه ولا يقال مكتسب فاتبع سبيل الحق واطرح الريب. حياته كذا الكلام السمع ثم البصر بذي أتانا السـ[؟]. فهل له إدراك أو لا خلف وعند قوم صح فيه الوقف. حي عليم قادر مريد سمع بصير ما يشا يريد.
Free from opposite, likeness, partner absolutely, and from parent, likewise the child, and the companions. Affirming Power, Will, and Knowledge as Distinct Eternal Attributes
The poet affirms power, will, and knowledge as distinct eternal attributes and warns against doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that power, will, and knowledge are real, distinct, and non-acquired attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's power, will, and knowledge are eternal, distinct, and not acquired.

After negating false concepts, the poet begins to affirm positive attributes. The first group includes power (qudra), will (irada), and knowledge ('ilm). He emphasizes that these are distinct from each other and that knowledge is not acquired. This brief affirmation carries profound theological implications for how we understand God's essence and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the attribute affirmation
Read 'وقدرة إرادة وغايرت وعلمه ولا يقال مكتسب' and note the order: power, will, knowledge. Also note the phrase 'غايرت' (they are different) and 'لا يقال مكتسب' (not said acquired).
The word 'غايرت' is past tense feminine plural, referring to the attributes being distinct from each other.
2
Analyze 'غايرت' and 'مكتسب'
'غايرت' indicates that power and will are different from each other and from the essence—they are not identical to the essence nor to each other. 'مكتسب' for knowledge means it is not obtained through learning or acquisition.
This refutes those who say attributes are identical to the essence or that knowledge is acquired.
3
Connect to the broader kalam context
In kalam, affirming attributes as distinct but not separate from essence is crucial. Why is it important that knowledge is not acquired? Because acquired knowledge implies dependence and change.
Acquired knowledge would make God temporal, but His knowledge is eternal and perfect.
4
Reflect on the poet's exhortation
The line ends with 'فاتبع سبيل الحق واطرح الريب' (so follow the path of truth and cast away doubts). This is a direct call to accept these theological affirmations.

Life, Speech, Hearing, Sight, and the Dispute on Perception
The poet affirms life, speech, hearing, sight, highlights a dispute on perception, and lists divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that life, speech, hearing, and sight are affirmed for Allah based on revelation, and you will be aware of the scholarly dispute regarding the attribute of perception (idrak).

Core Idea
Allah is living, speaking, hearing, and seeing—but whether He has perception is debated.

The poet continues by affirming four more attributes: life (hayat), speech (kalam), hearing (sam'), and sight (basar), noting that these come from revelation. Then a dispute emerges: does Allah have perception (idrak)? Some affirm it, while others suspend judgment (waqf). Finally, the passage concludes with a summary of divine names and the affirmation of Allah's absolute will.

Guided Path
1
Read the line on life and revelation-based attributes
Read 'حياته كذا الكلام السمع ثم البصر بذي أتانا السـ[؟]'. Note the missing word at the end; unclear but likely refers to revelation.
The phrase 'بذي أتانا' indicates these attributes are established by divine communication.
2
Understand the dispute on perception
Read the next phrase: 'فهل له إدراك أو لا خلف وعند قوم صح فيه الوقف'. This presents a difference: some say yes, some say stop (waqf).
Perception (idrak) is debated whether it is a separate attribute or included in knowledge.
3
Focus on the concluding divine names
The final line: 'حي عليم قادر مريد سمع بصير ما يشا يريد'. These are seven names/attributes: Living, Knowing, Powerful, Willing, Hearing, Seeing, and the affirmation that He does what He wills.
Note the order: the attributes of life and knowledge come first, then power and will, then hearing and sight, culminating in His absolute freedom to will.
4
Reflect on the significance of waqf
Why would some scholars suspend judgment on perception? This teaches that not all details of divine attributes have to be resolved; some matters can be left to God's knowledge alone.

Attributes of the Essence: Neither Other Nor Identical
Poet states rule on attributes of essence and applies to power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on divine attributes: they are neither separate from nor identical to the essence, and how divine power relates to all possible things without limit.

Core Idea
God's attributes are neither other than nor identical to His essence, but eternally subsisting in it.

This lesson examines a few lines from Jawharat al-Tawhid that clarify a subtle but crucial point of Ash'ari theology: the middle path between saying attributes are other than the essence or identical to it. The poet then turns to divine power (qudra) and its unlimited relation to all possible things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the poet's concise statement.
Notice the structure: first a general rule about attributes, then a specific example with qudra.
2
Understand 'neither other nor identical'
Ponder what it means for an attribute to be not other than the essence and not identical to it. This is the Ash'ari middle ground.
It denies both separation and pure identity, affirming that attributes are distinct in meaning but not in existence.
3
Apply the rule to divine power
See how the poet applies the general rule to the specific attribute of power: it is related to all possible things without end.
This shows the attribute's reality and its unlimited scope.
4
Reflect on the theological balance
Consider how this position avoids two extremes: likening God to creation (by saying attributes are other entities) and denying His perfections (by saying attributes are identical).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متكلم ثم صفات الذات ليست بغير أو بعين الذات. فقدرة بممكن تعلقت تعلقت بلا تناه ما به.
He is Mutakallim (Speaker); then the attributes of the essence are not other than nor identical to the essence. Then power (qudra) is related to the possible, related without end to what is by it (i.e., unlimited).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of divine attributes: they are neither other than the essence (ghayr) nor identical to it ('ayn). This is the Ash'ari position: attributes are not the essence nor other, but are eternally subsisting in the essence. Then the poet states that power (qudra) is related (muta'alliqa) to all possible things (mumkin) without limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are either separate entities from His essence or exactly identical to His essence.
Correction: Ash'ari theology takes a middle path: attributes are distinct in meaning but not separate in existence; they are not the essence nor other.

Checkpoint
How does the poet describe the relationship of the attributes of the essence to the essence?
A) They are other than the essence
B) They are identical to the essence
C) They are neither other nor identical
D) They are created

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are neither other than nor identical to the essence, and God's power extends to all possibles without limit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Ash'ari position guards against both stripping God of His attributes and likening Him to creation. In your own conception of God, have you ever fallen into one of these extremes? What will you correct today?

The Universal Scope of Divine Will and Knowledge
The poet states that divine will and knowledge encompass the necessary, possible, and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will and knowledge encompass not only the possible but also the necessary and the impossible.

Core Idea
God's will and knowledge cover everything conceivable.

This lesson unpacks a dense couplet from the Jawharah that establishes the all-encompassing nature of two key divine attributes. The poet asserts that will and knowledge extend to the necessary and the impossible, not just the actual. This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic lines carefully, noting the words 'عم' (encompasses) and the three categories: wajib, mumkin, mumtani' (implied).
The passage also mentions oneness and speech.
2
Identify the three categories of things
Recognize that 'واجبا' means the necessary and 'الممتنع' means the impossible. The possible is not explicitly mentioned but is understood from context.
In kalam, the possible is the default.
3
Understand 'عموم' (universality)
Note that the poet says 'عم' meaning they encompass. This is not limited to things that happen but includes everything.
4
Connect to divine speech
The poet says speech is similar in scope. Reflect on how God's speech relates to all things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدة أوجب لها ومثل ذي إرادة والعلم لكن عم ذي. وعم أيضًا واجبا والممتنع ومثل ذا كلامه فلنتبع. وكل موجود أنط للسمع به كذا البصر إدراكه إن قيل به. وغير علم هذه كما ثبت ثم الحياة ما بشي تعلقت.
And oneness—make it necessary for it, and like this are will and knowledge, but they encompass that. And they also encompass the necessary and the impossible; and like this is His speech, so let us follow. And every existing thing—rely for hearing on it, likewise sight, its perception if it is said. And other than knowledge these are as established, then life—it is not related to anything.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage discusses the universal scope of divine attributes. First, tawhid (oneness) is necessary. Then irada (will) and 'ilm (knowledge) are similar in that they encompass (amma) all things: the wajib (necessary), the mumtani' (impossible), and the mumkin (possible). Likewise, divine speech (kalam) encompasses. Then hearing (sam') and sight (basar) apply to every existing being; perception (idrak) is a disputed point. Then the poet notes that attributes other than knowledge are as established, and life (hayat) is not related to anything (i.e., it is not a relation like the others).

Key Terms
إرادة
علم
عموم
واجب
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's will and knowledge only apply to actual events.
Correction: The passage explicitly says they encompass the necessary and the impossible, not just the actual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what categories does divine will encompass?
A) Only actual things
B) The possible and actual
C) The necessary and the impossible
D) Only the possible

Key Takeaway
Divine will and knowledge are universal, encompassing the necessary, possible, and impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe God knows everything. But do you truly live as if He knows every intention you never acted on—every dark thought you dismissed? If He knows even the impossible possibilities, then nothing in your heart is hidden. How will this change what you allow yourself to dwell on today?
?
If God's will encompasses what never happens, then your excuses about 'I couldn't help it' collapse. There is always a possible path you did not choose. Which impossibility are you using to shield yourself from responsibility, and what will you do to face it knowing God's will covered that choice?
?
The poet says 'let us follow' after stating that divine speech encompasses all. If God's word speaks to every reality—even the impossible—then His Quran is sufficient for every condition you could face. What 'impossible' situation in your life have you been ignoring the Quran for, and will you open it today?

4

The poet says 'ثم الحياة ما بشي تعلقت' meaning life is not related to anything—it is not a relational attribute like knowledge or will.

Divine Names and Attributes Are Eternal
The poet affirms the eternity of divine names and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school affirms that God's names and attributes are eternal (qadima) and what this means.

Core Idea
God's names and attributes are eternal, not created.

The poet begins by affirming a fundamental tenet of Islamic theology: that Allah's names and attributes are not created or temporal but eternal. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the verse carefully: 'وعندنا أسماؤه العظيمة كذا صفات ذاته قديمة.' Then read its literal translation.
Focus on the word 'قديمة' (qadima).
2
Understand 'qadima'
Qadima means eternal or pre-eternal, not created in time. In Ash'ari theology, this affirms that God's names and attributes are necessary to His essence.
This opposes views that consider attributes as created or accidental.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider why this matters: if attributes were created, they would be contingent and God would not be eternally perfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا أسماؤه العظيمة كذا صفات ذاته قديمة. واختير أن أسماه توقيفية كذا الصفات فاحفظ السمعية. وكل نص أوهم التشبيها أوله أو فوض ورم تنزيها. ونزّه القرآن أي كلامه عن الحدوث واحذر انتقامة.
With us, His great names, likewise the attributes of His essence, are eternal. And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyya (derived from revelation), likewise the attributes—so preserve the revealed ones. And every text that suggests anthropomorphism (tashbih) – interpret it or consign (tafwid) and aspire to purify (tanzih). And purify the Quran, i.e., His speech, from temporality (huduth), and beware of its punishment.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage covers several points. First, God's names and attributes are qadima (eternal). Second, the naming (asma') is tawqifiyya, meaning they are only derived from revelation; we cannot name God by our own reasoning. Third, verses that seem to imply anthropomorphism (tashbih) should be either interpreted (ta'wil) or consigned (tafwid) while affirming tanzih (transcendence). Finally, the Quran is uncreated (not hadith) – it is God's speech – and one must beware of its punishment.

Key Terms
أسماء
صفات
قديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since names are revealed, they are created.
Correction: 'Qadima' refers to the attributes themselves, not the linguistic words. The names and attributes are eternal in the sense they are inherent to God's essence.

Checkpoint
What does 'qadima' mean in this context?
A) Created
B) Eternal
C) Recent
D) Temporal

Key Takeaway
God's names and attributes are eternal, not created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's attribute of 'knowing' is eternally aware of every thought you've ever had? If you truly believed that nothing escapes His eternal knowledge, what is one habit you would change starting today?
?
The poet affirms that God's attributes are eternal, not created. Is there an attribute of God—like His justice, mercy, or severity—that you subconsciously doubt is always true? How would your daily decisions shift if you committed to trusting that attribute as unchanging?
?
God's names are 'great' and eternal. Which name of God do you most neglect in your prayers or daily life? Make a concrete plan to invoke that name consciously each day this week, and ask yourself: what would it mean for this name to be truly eternal?

The poet states the preferred view on divine naming.

Core Idea
We only name God as He has named Himself.

Read the verse

Note the phrase 'احفظ السمعية' – preserve the revealed ones.
2
Understand 'tawqifiyya'
Tawqifiyya means derived from revelation; we cannot invent names for Allah based on reason alone.
This is the preferred Ash'ari view.
3
Consider the scope
The same principle applies to attributes: they are also tawqifiyya.
4
Implication
This safeguards against attributing inappropriate names to Allah and keeps theology anchored in revelation.

Dealing with Tashbih Verses and the Quran's Uncreatedness
The poet lays out the methodology for ambiguous verses and affirms Quran's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two valid approaches to ambiguous verses (ta'wil and tafwid) and why the Quran is uncreated.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses are either interpreted allegorically or consigned to God's knowledge, and the Quran is eternal speech.

The poet addresses a crucial issue: how to handle Quranic verses that seem to describe God in human-like terms. He presents two orthodox methods: ta'wil (interpretation) and tafwid (consignment). Then he affirms the uncreatedness of the Quran and warns against denying its punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read: 'وكل نص أوهم التشبيها أوله أو فوض ورم تنزيها. ونزّه القرآن أي كلامه عن الحدوث واحذر انتقامة.'
Note the two approaches: 'أوله' (interpret) and 'فوض' (consign).
2
Distinguish ta'wil and tafwid
Ta'wil assigns a figurative meaning consistent with tanzih; tafwid affirms the literal wording while leaving the true meaning to Allah.
Both are valid Sunni approaches.
3
Understand tanzih
The ultimate goal is tanzih (transcendence) – affirming that God is beyond any resemblance to creation.
4
Affirm Quran's uncreatedness
The Quran is God's speech and is not temporal (hadith); it is eternal. The warning refers to the punishment for denying this or for misinterpreting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

With us, His great names, likewise the attributes of His essence, are eternal. And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyya (derived from revelation), likewise the attributes—so preserve the revealed ones. And every text that suggests anthropomorphism (tashbih) – interpret it or consign (tafwid) and aspire to purify (tanzih). And purify the Quran, i.e., His speech, from temporality (huduth), and beware of its punishment.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage covers several points. First, God's names and attributes are qadima (eternal). Second, the naming (asma') is tawqifiyya, meaning they are only derived from revelation; we cannot name God by our own reasoning. Third, verses that seem to imply anthropomorphism (tashbih) should be either interpreted (ta'wil) or consigned (tafwid) while affirming tanzih (transcendence). Finally, the Quran is uncreated (not hadith) – it is God's speech – and one must beware of its punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Challenge yourself to honestly examine your reaction: are you more concerned with your own intellectual satisfaction or with preserving Allah's transcendence?

Reflect on one practical change you will make today to honor the Quran as Allah's eternal word.
?
The goal is 'tanzih' – purifying Allah from any imperfection. In your own life, are you actively striving to purify your understanding of Allah from any human-like attributes? Or have you unknowingly allowed subtle anthropomorphic ideas to linger in your heart? Ask yourself: if someone examined your beliefs about Allah's hearing and seeing, would they find that you truly believe they are unlike any human hearing and seeing? Divine Attributes

Divine Attributes of Essence and Their Eternity
Lines 32-38: enumeration of divine attributes of essence and their relationship to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attributes of essence are neither separate from nor identical to the essence, and they are co-eternal with Allah.

Core Idea
Attributes of essence are not other than nor identical to the essence; they are eternal.

In this lesson, we begin to explore the Ashʿari understanding of divine attributes. The poem lays down the foundational principle that attributes of essence have a unique relationship with the divine essence—neither fully distinct nor fully identical. This sets the stage for the list of seven attributes that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its translation. Let the rhythm of the poem and the author's terminology sink in.
Pay attention to the opening phrase about the attributes not being other nor identical.
2
Understand the middle position
Focus on the first line: 'صِفَاتُ الذَّاتِ لَيْسَتْ بِغَيْرِ أو بِعَيْنِ الذَّاتِ'. This is the key principle. Note that it rejects two extremes: attributes are not separate entities nor simply the essence without distinction.
This middle position is distinctive of Ashʿari theology.
3
Identify the listed attributes and their scopes
Go through each attribute mentioned: Power, Will, Knowledge, Speech, Hearing, Sight, Life. Notice how each is described—for example, Power relates to all possible things, Will and Knowledge encompass necessary, possible, and impossible.
The poem is compact; you may need to read each line multiple times to grasp the scope.
4
Conclude with eternity
The final line affirms that both the great names and the attributes of essence are eternal (qadīma). Reflect on why this is important: it confirms that the attributes are not created or temporal.
This ties the lesson to the broader theme of divine attributes being co-eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُتَكَلِّمْ ثُمَّ صِفَاتُ الذَّاتِ لَيْسَتْ بِغَيْرِ أو بِعَيْنِ الذَّاتِ . فَقُدْرَةٌ بِمُمْكِن تعلَّقَتْ تعلقت بِلا تَنَاهِي ما بِهِ . وَوَحْدَةً أَوْجِبْ لَها وَمِثْلُ دَيْ إرَادَةُ والعِلْمُ لَكِنْ عَمَّ ذِي . وعَمَّ أَيْضًا واجبًا والمُمْتَنِعْ ومِثْلُ ذَا كلامُهُ فَلْنَتَّبِع . وكلُّ موجودٍ أَنِطْ للسَّمـع بـة كَذَا البَصَرْ إِدْرَاكُهُ إِنْ قِيلَ بِه . وَغَيْرُ عِلْمٍ هَذِهِ كَمَا ثَبَتْ ثمَّ الحَيَاةُ ما بِشَيْ تَعَلَّقَتْ . وَعِنْدَنَا أَسماؤُهُ العَظِيمَة كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذاتِهِ قَدِيمَة .
He is Mutakallim (Speaker). Then the attributes of essence are not other than nor identical to the essence. Power is related to the possible, related without limit to what is by it. And attribute unity (oneness) to it, and like it will and knowledge, but they encompass that (all objects). And they also encompass the necessary and the impossible; and like this is His Speech, so let us follow. And every existent is subjected to hearing and likewise sight, its perception if it is said by it. And other than knowledge, these are as established, then life which is not related to anything. And with us His great names, likewise the attributes of His essence, are eternal.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the divine attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) as presented in the poem. The author first states the rule: these attributes are neither other than the essence nor identical to it in a way that would negate distinction—they are not separate entities nor simply the essence itself. Then he lists specific attributes: Power (qudra) which relates to all possible things without limit; Unity (waḥda) which must be affirmed along with Will (irāda) and Knowledge (ʿilm), and these three encompass all objects—necessary, possible, and impossible. Similarly, Speech (kalām) is treated the same. Then hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar) are mentioned as perceptions of existents. Then other attributes like life (ḥayāh) which does not relate to any particular thing. Finally, he concludes by affirming both the great names and the attributes of essence are eternal (qadīma). This establishes the Ashʿari position on divine attributes as co-eternal with the essence, neither created nor accidental.

Key Terms
صفات الذات
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
الكلام
السمع
البصر
الحياة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributes are either entirely other than the essence or entirely identical to it.
Correction: The correct Ashʿari position is a middle way: attributes are neither other nor identical.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, what is the relationship of the attributes of essence to the essence itself?
A) They are separate from the essence.
B) They are identical to the essence.
C) They are not other than nor identical to the essence.
D) They are created entities.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of essence are co-eternal with Allah and are neither separate from nor identical to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's power is eternal and limitless, why do you sometimes doubt His ability to change your situation? What specific circumstance are you holding back from trusting Him with?
?
You call upon Al-ʿAlīm (the All-Knowing) daily—but do you truly live as if He knows every secret thought and intention? What is one thing you try to hide, and how can you surrender it to His eternal knowledge today?
?
The attributes of His essence are co-eternal with Him—He has always been hearing your prayers and seeing your tears. How does this reality confront your impatience when you feel unheard? What step will you take to deepen your certainty that He hears you right now?

Divine Names Are Tawqifiyyah
Ruling on divine names being tawqifiyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the names of Allah must be derived from revelation and not from human reasoning.

Core Idea
Allah's names are not invented; they are revealed.

This passage establishes a fundamental principle in Islamic theology: the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah, meaning they are based on divine revelation. Before we can speak about Allah, we must know which names are authentically His.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic phrase 'واختيرَ أَنَّ اسْماهُ تَوْقِيفِيَّةً كذَا الصِّفات فاحفظ السَّمْعِيَّة' and its English translation. Notice that it starts with 'and it is chosen', indicating a preferred view.
The term 'اختير' suggests there is a difference of opinion, but the chosen view is stated.
2
Understand the term 'tawqifiyyah'
Tawqifiyyah means that something is based on revelation (waqf) from Allah. The names of Allah are not subject to human reasoning or ijtihad; they are taken directly from the Quran and Sunnah.
The same rule applies to attributes as stated: 'كذَا الصِّفات'.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'fahfaz al-samʿiyyah'
The phrase 'so preserve the auditory' emphasizes that you must adhere strictly to what is heard (i.e., revealed). This guards against innovation in naming Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختيرَ أَنَّ اسْماهُ تَوْقِيفِيَّةً كذَا الصِّفات فاحفظ السَّمْعِيَّة . وكُلُّ نَصَّ أَوهَمَ التَّشْبِيها أَوَّلُهُ أَو فَوَّض ورُمْ تَنْزِيهَا .
And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah (derived from revelation), likewise the attributes, so preserve the auditory (revelation-based). And every text that suggests similarity, either interpret it or consign (its meaning to Allah) and seek to declare Him transcendent.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage provides two rulings. First, the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah, meaning they are not established by human reasoning but must be taken from revelation (samʿiyyah). The same applies to attributes. Second, regarding ambiguous verses (mutashābihāt) that seem to imply anthropomorphism (tashbīh), the correct approach is either ta’wīl (interpretation that befits Allah) or tafwīḍ (consigning the inner meaning to Allah), while always affirming transcendence (tanzīh). This is the standard Ashʿari methodology.

Key Terms
توقيفية
السمعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any name can be used for Allah as long as it is respected and has a good meaning.
Correction: Only names explicitly mentioned in revelation are permissible. Naming Allah by human invention is not allowed because it implies knowledge of His essence without divine guidance.

Checkpoint
What does 'tawqifiyyah' mean in the context of Allah's names?
A) They are based on human reason.
B) They are based on revelation.
C) They are known by consensus.
D) They are all found in the Quran only.

Key Takeaway
The names of Allah are only known through revelation, not human reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever refer to Him by a name or description that you have not found in the Quran or Sunnah? Where did that idea come from? This lesson warns that His names are only from revelation. Will you commit to checking every name you use for Allah against the revealed texts?
?
If someone told you that Allah can be called 'The Wise' (al-Hakim) but also 'The Intelligent' since intelligence is similar, how would you respond using this passage? Are you willing to reject even a seemingly good name if it is not revealed? Reflect on whether you have been using any 'good-sounding' names for Allah without evidence.
?
The command 'so preserve the revelation-based' (فاحفظ السَّمْعِيَّة) is directed at you. What does it mean to 'preserve' in your heart? How can you actively protect the way you speak about Allah from innovations? Write down one practical step you will take today to guard your tongue regarding Allah's names and attributes.

Treatment of Ambiguous Verses
Directive on handling ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two correct approaches to verses that seem to imply anthropomorphism: interpretation or consignment.

Core Idea
When verses seem to suggest similarity, we either interpret or entrust the meaning to Allah.

The Quran contains verses that, if taken literally, might suggest Allah resembles creation. The scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah have two safe methods: ta’wīl (interpretation) or tafwīḍ (consignment), both while affirming transcendence. This lesson explains that directive.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read 'وكُلُّ نَصَّ أَوهَمَ التَّشْبِيها أَوَّلُهُ أَو فَوَّض ورُمْ تَنْزِيهَا' and its translation. Note that it covers 'every text' that gives the impression of similarity (tashbīh).
The word 'أوهم' means 'suggests' or 'gives the illusion of' – it does not mean actually implying.
2
Understand the two options
The passage gives two options: 'awwilhu' (interpret it) or 'fawwiḍ' (consign its meaning to Allah). Both are legitimate and accepted by the Salaf. The first involves reinterpretation, the second is a complete entrustment.
Some scholars prefer tafwīḍ for most verses, while others prefer ta’wīl with proper conditions.
3
Emphasize tanzīh
The phrase 'wa rum tanzīhā' means 'and seek to declare Him transcendent.' In both approaches, the goal is to preserve Allah's transcendence above any resemblance to creation.
This is the essential principle: no matter the method, tanzīh must be maintained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختيرَ أَنَّ اسْماهُ تَوْقِيفِيَّةً كذَا الصِّفات فاحفظ السَّمْعِيَّة . وكُلُّ نَصَّ أَوهَمَ التَّشْبِيها أَوَّلُهُ أَو فَوَّض ورُمْ تَنْزِيهَا .
And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah (derived from revelation), likewise the attributes, so preserve the auditory (revelation-based). And every text that suggests similarity, either interpret it or consign (its meaning to Allah) and seek to declare Him transcendent.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage provides two rulings. First, the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah, meaning they are not established by human reasoning but must be taken from revelation (samʿiyyah). The same applies to attributes. Second, regarding ambiguous verses (mutashābihāt) that seem to imply anthropomorphism (tashbīh), the correct approach is either ta’wīl (interpretation that befits Allah) or tafwīḍ (consigning the inner meaning to Allah), while always affirming transcendence (tanzīh). This is the standard Ashʿari methodology.

Key Terms
أوهم التشبيه
أوله
فوض
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ambiguous verses must be taken literally without any qualification.
Correction: They require either interpretation or consignment, never literal anthropomorphism, because that would imply a created being like creation.

Checkpoint
What are the two acceptable approaches to ambiguous verses that suggest similarity?
A) Literal interpretation and denial.
B) Interpretation (ta’wīl) and consignment (tafwīḍ).
C) Rejection and speculation.
D) Analogy and metaphor.

Key Takeaway
When facing verses that seem to suggest similarity to creation, we either interpret them appropriately or entrust their meaning to Allah, always affirming His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse about Allah's attributes and felt a sense of unease because it seemed to imply something physical? In that moment, did you choose ta'wil or tafwid? Be honest with yourself: were you truly affirming tanzih, or did you let your imagination form a mental image?
?
When you consign the meaning of a verse to Allah (tafwid), does your heart find genuine peace, or do you feel a lingering curiosity to 'know'? Are you willing to leave that knowledge with Him, trusting that He knows best?
?
The poem gives two options: interpret or consign. Which one do you rely on more, and is that choice based on humility or on intellectual pride? Ask yourself: are you seeking to protect Allah's transcendence, or are you protecting your own understanding?

The Quran Is Uncreated and Allah Is Not in a Direction
Affirming the Quran's eternity and denying direction for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah and why Allah is not confined by direction, based on the theological principle that attributes of perfection cannot have opposites.

Core Idea
The Quran is eternal, and Allah is beyond spatial limits.

This passage from al-Jawhara addresses two fundamental beliefs: the uncreatedness of the Quran and the impossibility of Allah being in a direction. The author presents a hermeneutical rule for interpreting ambiguous texts and reaffirms the transcendence of Allah's attributes. Understanding these points is essential for correct Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation without trying to analyze. Let the author's own rhythm come through first.
2
Identify the two rulings
Notice how the passage first commands regarding the Quran, then gives a rule for interpretation, and finally states impossibility of direction. These are the two main doctrines taught here.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why createdness and direction are opposites of divine attributes, and why they are impossible for Allah. The commentary will help clarify this.
This connects to the principle that Allah's attributes are perfect and their opposites are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونزّه القرآن أي كلامه عنِ الحُدوتِ واحْذَرِ انتقامة . فكلُّ نصَّ لِلْحُدُوثِ دَلَّا احْمِلْ عَلَى اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي قَدْ دَلَّا . ويستحِيلُ ضِدُّ ذي الصِّفاتِ في حَقّه كالكون في الجهات .
And declare the Quran, i.e., His speech, transcendent above originatedness, and beware of its denial. So every text that indicates originatedness, interpret it according to the wording that actually indicates (the correct meaning). And the opposite of the possessor of attributes is impossible in His right, like being in directions.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defends the uncreatedness of the Quran. The author commands to declare the Quran transcendent above ḥudūth (originatedness in time) and warns against denying its eternal nature. Then he gives a hermeneutical rule: any scriptural text that seems to suggest the Quran is created (or originated) should be interpreted in line with the established textual evidence that affirms its eternity. Finally, he states that any opposite of the divine attributes is impossible for Allah—for example, being confined to directions (jihāt), which would imply corporeality. This is a standard refutation of the Muʿtazila (who held the Quran is created) and the anthropomorphists.

Key Terms
القرآن
كلامه
الحدوث
الجهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is a created entity and Allah is confined to a direction (e.g., sitting on the Throne in a literal sense).
Correction: The Quran is the eternal, uncreated speech of Allah, and Allah transcends all spatial directions; any verse suggesting direction is interpreted metaphorically.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what should be done with a text that suggests the Quran is created?
A) Accept it literally.
B) Interpret it according to the wording that indicates the correct meaning.
C) Reject the text altogether.
D) Consign its meaning to Allah only.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah, and Allah is free from all spatial limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited, do you feel in your heart that this is the eternal speech of Allah, or does your mind slip into treating it like ordinary human speech? What would it mean for your awe and reverence to truly internalise its uncreatedness, and how can you actively cultivate that conviction today?
?
You have learned that Allah is beyond all directions and places. Reflect for a moment: have you ever internally pictured Allah as 'up above' or 'in a particular spot' during du'a or worship? If so, what practical step will you take to purge that image and instead affirm His transcendence, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
This passage warns to beware of denying the Quran's eternity and Allah's freedom from direction. Are there any modern ideas—from science, culture, or your own doubts—that subtly make you question these truths? Will you commit to studying the arguments of Ahl al-Sunnah so that your heart finds rest in the creed of tanzīh?

The Possible Attributes of Allah: Creation and Destruction
A single line defining the possible (jā'iz) attributes of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's possible actions include all things that are inherently possible, such as creating and destroying, and that He is not compelled to act in any particular way.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely free to create or not create; nothing is forced upon Him.

This short line from the Jawharat al-Tawhid poem introduces the category of 'possible' (jā'iz) with respect to Allah. It clarifies that actions which are inherently possible—like creating something or causing it to cease—are permissible for Allah. The example of granting wealth illustrates how Allah may or may not bestow favors. This concept safeguards against the misconception that Allah is obligated to create or to act in a certain way.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic line carefully.
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms: جائز, ما أمكننا, إيجادًا, إعدامًا, رزق الغنى.
The line is part of a longer poem on divine attributes.
2
Understand the meaning of 'jā'iz' for Allah.
Possible (jā'iz) in this context means an action that Allah may do or not do without it being necessary or impossible. It is contrasted with the necessary (wājib) and the impossible (mustaḥīl).
Allah’s possible actions are those that are ontologically possible in themselves.
3
Analyze the example: granting wealth.
The line gives the example of Allah granting wealth (rizq al-ghinā). This shows that while Allah can give wealth, He is not obliged to do so; it is a possible action.
This example reinforces that all created things are contingent and depend on Allah's free will.
4
Reflect on the theological implication.
This teaching frees Allah from any sense of compulsion. He creates and destroys as He wills, and no being can demand anything from Him.
It counters the misconception that Allah must reward or punish in a particular way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجائز فـي حقـه مـا أَمْكَنَا إيجادًا إِعْدَامًا كَرَزْقِهِ الغِنَى .
And permissible in His right is what is possible for us, in terms of bringing into existence or non-existence, like His granting of wealth.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This single line explains the category of jā’iz (possible) with respect to Allah. It states that whatever is possible (mumkin) in itself—like bringing into existence or causing non-existence—is permissible for Allah to do. The example given is giving wealth (rizq al-ghinā). This is the standard division: Allah has necessary attributes, impossible attributes, and possible actions. This line covers the possible actions of Allah, affirming His absolute freedom to create or not create.

Key Terms
جائز (jā'iz)
إيجاد (ījād)
إعدام (iʿdām)
رزق الغنى (rizq al-ghinā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah is forced to create or to carry out specific actions.
Correction: Allah is absolutely free; all created things and actions are possible for Him, and He may do or not do them as He wills.

Checkpoint
What does the poem say is permissible (jā'iz) for Allah?
A) Only the necessary.
B) Whatever is possible for us, like bringing into existence or annihilating.
C) Only that which benefits us.
D) Nothing beyond the necessary.

Key Takeaway
Allah’s possible actions include all inherently possible acts, such as creating and destroying, because He is free to act without compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is absolutely free to grant wealth or withhold it, is your sense of security rooted in the wealth itself or in the One who can give or take it? Today, consciously acknowledge that everything you own is a gift that could be removed at any moment.
?
When you face a painful loss—whether of a person, health, or status—do you truly believe that Allah was free to bring that non-existence into being? How can you cultivate a heart that submits to His freedom even when it hurts?
?
This lesson teaches that creation and destruction are mere possibilities for Allah; only He is necessary. What would change in your daily routine if you truly lived as though nothing in this world is necessary but Allah?

Defining Provision (Rizq)
Two verses defining rizq and stating Allah's provision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of rizq (provision) and that Allah provides both lawful and unlawful things.

Core Idea
Rizq includes both beneficial and harmful, lawful and unlawful.

Sanusi begins by clarifying what is meant by 'provision' (rizq). He presents two definitions from scholars and then asserts that Allah provides all types, including the disliked and forbidden. This lesson unpacks those definitions and their theological implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses
Read verses 119-120 carefully. Notice the two definitions and the conclusion about what Allah provides.
Verse 119 has an extra period after the number; ignore formatting.
2
Identify the two definitions
The first definition says rizq is anything beneficial. The second says it is what is owned and pursued. Which one do you think is more common?
3
Understand Allah's provision
Verse 120 affirms that Allah provides lawful, disliked, and forbidden things. This means rizq is not restricted to the lawful.
Some may mistakenly believe rizq is only lawful; this verse corrects that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٩ - . والرِّزْقُ عِنْدَ القَوْمِ مَا بِهِ انْتُفِعْ وقيل لا بل ما مُلِكْ وما اتَّبِعْ ١٢٠ - فيرزُقُ الله الحلال فاعْلَمَا ويرزُقُ المكرُوهَ وَالمُحَرَّما ١٢١ - في الاكتسابِ والتَّوَكَّلِ اخْتُلِفْ والرَّاجِعُ التَّفْصِيلُ حَسْبَما عُرِفْ ١٢٢ - وعندنا الشَّيء هو الموجود - وثابت في الخارج الموجود ١٢٣ - وجودُ شَيءٍ عينه والجَوهَرُ الفرد حادث عندنا لا يُنْكَرُ
119 - And provision according to the people is that from which benefit is taken. Some said: no, rather it is what is owned and followed. 120 - So Allah provides the lawful, know, and He provides the disliked and the forbidden. 121 - Concerning acquisition and trust, they differed, and the correct view is the detail according to what is known. 122 - And with us, the thing is the existent, and it is established in external existence. 123 - The existence of a thing is its very self, and the individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) is originated, according to us, not denied.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage covers theological and metaphysical topics. Verse 119 gives two definitions of rizq (provision): some say it is whatever is beneficial, others say it is what one owns and pursues. Verse 120 clarifies that Allah provides both lawful and unlawful things. Verse 121 mentions differing views on acquisition (al-iktisāb) and trust in God (al-tawakkul), stating the correct view is the detailed one based on known principles. Verse 122 defines 'thing' as the existent that is established in external reality. Verse 123 asserts that the existence of a thing is identical to its essence, and that the individual substance (the atom) is originated (ḥādith), not eternal, and this is not denied.

Key Terms
الرزق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rizq is only what is lawful and good.
Correction: Verse 120 states that Allah provides the disliked and forbidden as well, so rizq includes both lawful and unlawful.

Checkpoint
According to verse 120, which of the following does Allah provide?
A) Only the lawful
B) Only the unlawful
C) The lawful, the disliked, and the forbidden
D) Only what is beneficial

Key Takeaway
Rizq is broader than just the lawful; Allah provides all sustenance, whether beneficial or harmful, lawful or forbidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one thing you consumed today that was not fully halal. Did you consider it a provision from Allah even though it was forbidden? How will you change your intake tomorrow knowing that Allah provides both halal and haram, but you are accountable for which you choose?
?
Which definition of rizq do you live by—benefit or ownership? Are you more focused on owning or benefiting from what Allah gives? Commit to reassessing your priorities this week based on this lesson.
?
If Allah provides the disliked and the forbidden, does that absolve you of responsibility? Or does it increase your need to seek His guidance on what to accept? Write down one step you will take today to ensure your rizq is halal.

Acquisition and Trust in God
Single verse on differing views of acquisition and trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing views on acquisition (iktisab) and trust (tawakkul) and the correct position according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
The correct view on acquisition and trust is the detailed one based on known principles.

Verse 121 touches on a debated topic in kalam: how humans acquire actions and how they rely on Allah. Sanusi states that the correct position is not a simple either/or but a detailed answer that depends on known principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse
Read verse 121. Notice it mentions two concepts: acquisition (al-iktisāb) and trust (al-tawakkul).
2
Understand the disagreement
Scholars differed on the nature of iktisab (how human action relates to divine power) and tawakkul (reliance on Allah).
These are important Ash'ari concepts.
3
Learn the correct view
The verse says the correct view is 'the detail according to what is known' – meaning a nuanced position taking into account known principles from revelation and reason.
Sanusi alludes to the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb.

Verse 120 clarifies that Allah provides both lawful and unlawful things. Verse 121 mentions differing views on acquisition (al-iktisāb) and trust in God (al-tawakkul), stating the correct view is the detailed one based on known principles. Verse 122 defines 'thing' as the existent that is established in external reality. Verse 123 asserts that the existence of a thing is identical to its essence, and that the individual substance (the atom) is originated (ḥādith), not eternal, and this is not denied.

Key Terms

التوكل

Key Takeaway
The correct understanding of acquisition and trust requires a nuanced, detailed approach grounded in revealed and rational knowledge.

?

Existence and the Atom
Two verses on thing, existence, and the individual substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of 'thing' as existent, the identity of existence and essence, and the origination of the individual substance.

Core Idea
Thing means existent; existence is identical to essence; the atom is created.

Sanusi presents core metaphysical doctrines of Ash'arism: 'thing' (shay') is synonymous with 'existent' and is established externally; the existence of a thing is its very essence (not an accident); and the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) is originated, not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 122-123
Read both verses together. Notice the definition of shay' and the statement about existence and essence.
2
Define 'thing'
Verse 122 says that according to us (Ash'aris), 'thing' is the existent that is established externally.
This rejects the Mu'tazili view that 'thing' includes nonexistents.
3
Identify existence-essence identity
Verse 123 says the existence of a thing is its very self (aynuh). Existence is not a separate attribute.
This is a key Ash'ari position.
4
Note the atom's origination
The individual substance (jawhar fard) is originated (hadith), meaning created in time, not eternal.
This opposes the philosophers who considered atoms eternal.

Understanding Rizq: Definition and Divine Provision
Defines rizq and states that Allah provides both halal and haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of rizq mentioned by Sanusi and the key point that Allah provides both lawful and unlawful things.

Core Idea
Rizq includes both halal and haram; Allah provides all.

This lesson explores the Islamic concept of provision (rizq). The author presents two definitions and then confirms that Allah is the provider of all things, whether lawful, disliked, or forbidden. This clarifies a common theological point about divine provision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is in verse form.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage presents two views of rizq: one based on benefit, another based on ownership. Find each and note how they differ.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider what it means that Allah provides even the disliked and forbidden. This refutes the idea that haram is not from Allah's provision.
This connects to the concept of divine will and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرِّزْقُ عِنْدَ القَوْمِ مَا بِهِ انْتُفِعْ وقيل لا بل ما مُلِكْ وما اتَّبِعْ فيرزُقُ الله الحلال فاعْلَمَا ويرزُقُ المكرُوهَ وَالمُحَرَّما في الاكتسابِ والتَّوَكَّلِ اخْتُلِفْ والرَّاجِعُ التَّفْصِيلُ حَسْبَما عُرِفْ
