So Allah provides the halal, so know, and He provides the disliked and the forbidden. In acquisition and tawakkul, there is difference, and the preferable is the detail according to what is known.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of provision (rizq). First, it mentions the common view that rizq is anything that one derives benefit from. Then it presents an alternative view: rizq is only what one actually possesses (mulk) and what follows (perhaps what one acquires through permissible means). Then it affirms that Allah is the provider of all things, including both halal and haram, meaning that even forbidden things are provided by Allah in the sense of being brought into existence. Finally, it notes a disagreement regarding acquisition (iktisab) and tawakkul (reliance on God), stating that the correct position is to differentiate (tafsil) based on what is known (i.e., the detailed rulings of Shari'ah).

الاكتساب (acquisition)

الحلال (the lawful)
المكرُوه (the disliked)
المُحَرَّم (the forbidden)
التَّفْصِيل (differentiation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Haram things are not provided by Allah.
Correction: The text affirms that Allah provides the haram, meaning He creates it and allows acquisition, though it is forbidden to consume.

Checkpoint

B) What is owned and what is followed
C) What is beneficial and what is owned

Key Takeaway
Rizq includes both lawful and unlawful things; Allah provides all, but the details of acquisition and reliance require differentiation.

Definitions of Allah, al-Rahman, and al-Rahim
Lexical definitions of the divine names Allah, al-Rahman, and al-Rahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical definitions of three key divine names: Allah, al-Rahman, and al-Rahim, and the distinction between the types of blessings each denotes.

Core Idea
Allah is a proper name for the Necessary Being; al-Rahman and al-Rahim describe His blessings.

The basmala begins with three of Allah's most beautiful names. Before we can appreciate their theological significance, we need to know what they mean at the lexical level. This short passage from al-Sanusi gives precise definitions that distinguish these names from one another.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice that each name is defined concisely. Then read the English translation.
Pay attention to the terms 'جَلَائِل' and 'دَقَائِق'.
2
Understand the definition of 'Allah'
The passage defines 'Allah' as a proper name for the self that is necessary of existence. Reflect on why this definition matters for tawhid.
3
Distinguish between al-Rahman and al-Rahim
Compare the two names. Both involve bestowing blessings, but one is for great blessings and the other for subtle ones. Consider how this distinction relates to Allah's mercy.
This distinction is traditional in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله : علَمٌ ، على الذات الواجب الوجود . والرَّحمن: المُنْعِم بجلائل النعم . والرَّحيم : المُنْعِم بدقائقها .
And Allah: a proper name for the self that is necessary of existence. And al-Rahman: the Bestower of great blessings. And al-Rahim: the Bestower of subtle blessings.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage provides lexical definitions of three of the most important divine names in the basmala. 'Allah' is defined as a proper name (alam) referring to the self that is necessarily existent (al-dhat al-wajib al-wujud). This means it is not a mere attribute or description but a unique name indicating a specific entity. 'Al-Rahman' is defined as the one who bestows great and momentous blessings (jila'il al-ni'am). 'Al-Rahim' is defined as the one who bestows subtle and fine blessings (daqaiqiha). This distinction between the scope of mercy is traditional: al-Rahman implies universal mercy, while al-Rahim implies specific mercy to believers, but here the focus is on the magnitude of blessings.

Correction: The passage clearly states it is a proper name (alam), not derived. This is the position of many theologians.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Word 'Allah' as a Proper Name and Its Implication for Tawhid
Proof that 'Allah' is a proper name using the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'Allah' must be a proper name and how this supports the shahada's exclusive indication of tawhid.

Core Idea
'Allah' is not a generic term; it is the unique personal name of the Creator.

This lesson examines a foundational claim in Islamic theology: that the word 'Allah' (لفظ الجلالة) is a personal proper name, not a common noun. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how the testimony of faith, 'La ilaha illa Allah', uniquely singles out Allah for worship. The passage presents a logical proof based on the consensus of Muslims and monotheists on the exclusive meaning of the kalima.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the full argument. Let the author's wording sink in before analysis.
Notice the density of the argument; each sentence builds on the last.
2
Identify the claim and its explanation
Find where the author states that the chosen view is that 'Allah' is a personal proper name (علم شخصي). Read the explanation: the referent is specific in the external world, not due to attributes.
The author excludes accidental attributes like whiteness or tallness because they are impossible for Allah.
3
Understand the proof
Follow the argument: if 'Allah' were a universal term (like a common noun), then the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah' would not exclusively indicate tawhid. Why? Because it could mean 'no god except a certain type of divine being'.
The proof hinges on the meaning of the shahada being exclusive.
4
See the role of consensus
The author cites unanimous agreement among Muslims and even monotheists across religions that the kalima indicates tawhid. This consensus is used to support the argument.
Consensus (ijma') is a key source of evidence in kalam.
5
Conclude and reflect
The conclusion: 'Allah' must be a proper name indicating a specific meaning. Reflect on how this strengthens the concept of tawhid and the uniqueness of Allah.
This is a classic kalam argument for the proper name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشير إلى أنَّ المُختار أنَّ لفظ الجلالة علمٌ شخصي، بمعنى أن مدلوله معين في الخارج، لا أَنَّه قامت به مُشخصات كالبياض والطول ؛ لأنَّ ذلك المعنى مستحيل بالنسبة الله الله بمعنى والمقصود من هذا أن لفظ الجلالة ليس دالا على معنى كُليّ ؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك لم تكن كلمة التوحيد ـ وهي قولنا لا إله إلا الله» دالة على إفراده سبحانه بالألوهية دون سائر من عداه ، وقد أجمع المسلمون ، بل الموحدون من جميع الملل في جميع أَنَّ هذه الكلمة دالة على التّوحيد، ومن أجل ذلك سُمِّيت كلمة التوحيد)، فوجب ألا يكون لفظ الجلالة دالا على معنى كلي، فثبت أنه دال على معنى مُشخّص بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه .
He indicates that the chosen view is that the word of majesty is a personal proper name, meaning that its referent is specific in the external world, not that it has identifying attributes like whiteness and tallness because that meaning is impossible with respect to Allah. And the intent of this is that the word of majesty does not indicate a universal meaning; because if it were so, the word of tawhid — which is our saying 'There is no god but Allah' — would not indicate His being singled out for divinity to the exclusion of others. And the Muslims, rather the monotheists of all religions in all ages, have unanimously agreed that this word indicates tawhid, and for that reason it is called the kalima al-tawhid. Therefore it is necessary that the word of majesty does not indicate a universal meaning, so it is established that it indicates a specific meaning in the sense we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument that the word 'Allah' (lafz al-jalala) is a proper name (alam shakhsi) and not a common noun. The author begins by stating the preferred view: it is a personal proper name, meaning its referent is a specific entity in the external world, not that it is distinguished by accidental attributes like whiteness or height, as that is impossible for God. The proof is that if 'Allah' were a universal term (like 'god' meaning a divine being), then the testimony of faith 'La ilaha illa Allah' would not be exclusive to God, because it could mean 'no god except a certain type of divine being'. But Muslims and monotheists across all religions agree that the kalima indicates tawhid, the singling out of Allah alone for divinity. Therefore, 'Allah' must be a proper name that refers directly to the specific divine being, not a common noun. This is a kalam argument for the uniqueness of God's name.

Key Terms
لفظ الجلالة
علم شخصي
كلمة التوحيد
لا إله إلا الله
معنى كلي
معنى مشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Allah' is a generic term meaning 'God', like a common noun applicable to any deity.
Correction: The passage proves that 'Allah' is a proper name, uniquely referring to the one true God, which is why the shahada exclusively indicates tawhid.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is derived from a root

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that 'Allah' is a proper name, ensuring the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah' exclusively refers to Allah, which is the foundation of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Balanced Definition of Tawhid
Presents a second definition of Tawhid that refutes both anthropomorphism and negation of attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of Tawhid that affirms Allah's essence as incomparable yet not devoid of attributes.

Core Idea
Tawhid balances between denying resemblance and denying attributes.

The first definition of Tawhid often focuses on oneness. But here we encounter a definition that directly guards against two dangerous extremes: anthropomorphism (tashbih) and stripping Allah of His attributes (ta'til). This short but powerful statement sets the Sunni middle path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and English versions. Notice how the definition is introduced with 'وَقِيلَ' indicating it is an alternative view.
The definition is concise yet comprehensive.
2
Identify the two negations
The definition has two parts: affirming an essence that is 'غير مُشبَّهة' (not resembling) other essences, and 'لا مُعطَّلةٍ عن الصفات' (not devoid of attributes). These negate tashbih and ta'til respectively.
3
Reflect on the middle path
Consider why both negations are necessary. Denying only one leads to imbalance. The Sunni position avoids both extremes.
This definition is a key formulation in Ilm al-Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقِيلَ : التَّوْحِيدُ : إثبات ذات غير مُشبَّهة بالذُّواتِ، ولا مُعطَّلةٍ عن الصفات
And it is said: Tawhid is: affirming an essence not resembling other essences, nor devoid of attributes.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides an alternative definition of Tawhid: it is affirming an essence that does not resemble other essences (refuting tashbih/anthropomorphism) nor is it devoid of attributes (refuting ta'til/negation of attributes). This definition balances between two extremes and is crucial for Sunni creed.

Key Terms
إثبات ذات
غير مشبهة
معطلة
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that Tawhid only means affirming one essence without attributes (ta'til) or that it means comparing God to creation (tashbih).
Correction: The second definition explicitly guards against both extremes by affirming an essence that is neither resembling nor devoid of attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the second definition of tawhid guard against?
A) Only tashbih
B) Only ta'til
C) Both tashbih and ta'til
D) Shirk

Key Takeaway
Tawhid requires affirming Allah's essence as unique and possessing attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your heart, do you tend to imagine Allah in a human-like form (tashbih) or avoid thinking of His attributes altogether (ta'til)? Which extreme is your default, and what specific step will you take today to embrace the balanced understanding of Tawhid taught in this lesson?
?
When you encounter attributes like 'hand' or 'face' in the Quran, do you feel uncomfortable and tempted to explain them away (ta'til)? How will you train yourself to affirm them without resemblance, as this definition commands?
?
The definition requires affirming an essence that does not resemble anything. Does your daily worship reflect this truth, or do you unconsciously picture God? Write one concrete change you will make in your du'a or dhikr to remind yourself of His uniqueness.

Refutation of Philosophers and Mu'tazila on Divine Reward and Obligation
Commentator refutes philosophers and Mu'tazila on reward and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari commentator refutes both the philosophers (who affirm spiritual reward but deny bodily resurrection) and the Mu'tazila (who claim Allah must do the most beneficial) using the concepts of 'obligation' (ijab) and 'necessity' (wujub).

Core Idea
Reward does not arise by obligation; Allah is free from compulsion.

This passage clarifies the Ash'ari stance on divine reward: it flows from Allah's essence freely, not by external compulsion. The commentator uses the example of a ring moving with the finger to illustrate causation without obligation. He then addresses two opposing groups: philosophers, who deny bodily resurrection but affirm spiritual reward, and Mu'tazila, who assert moral obligation upon Allah. Both refutations hinge on the rejection of any necessitating factor upon God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'ijab'
Read the commentator's explanation that 'obligation' here means causation. Reward arises from Allah's essence necessarily, like a ring's movement from a finger's movement.
2
Identify the first refutation
Notice the phrase 'without obligation' is a refutation of the philosophers. They deny any reward originally, but actually affirm spiritual reward. The commentator anticipates an objection and answers it.
The objection is: how can it refute them if they deny reward altogether? The answer: they only deny bodily resurrection but affirm spiritual reward.
3
Identify the second refutation
The phrase 'without necessity' refutes the Mu'tazila who claim Allah must do what is most beneficial. The Ash'ari position rejects any obligation upon Allah.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how these refutations defend divine freedom and sovereignty. Reward and punishment are acts of Allah's will, not compelled by external factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الإيجاب هنا التعليل، أي : أنَّ ثواب الله تعالى ينشأ عن ذاته قهرا كحركة الخاتم النَّاشئةِ عن حركة الإصبع بطريق التعليل ، وقد أشار بقوله : (من غير إيجاب» إلى الرَّد على الفلاسفة، فإن قلت: كيف يكون قوله : مِن غير إيجاب» ردًّا على الفلاسفة أنَّ الفلاسفة ينكرونَ مع أصله ، وذلك يستلزم أنهم لا يثبتون ثواباً أصلا لا بالإيجاب ولا بغيره ؟ والجواب عن هذا أنهم ينكرون حشر الأجسام، ويقولون بحشر الأرواح، وعندهم الأرواح تُثاب باللذات المعنوية وتعاقب بالمُنَفِّصَات والمُكَدِّرات المعنوية أيضا، وأشار بقوله: (ولا وجوب إلى الرَّدِّ على المعتزلة القائلين بوجوب الصلاح والأصلح على الله تعالى ، وسيأتي الرَّدُّ على الفريقين إن شاء الله تعالى.
The meaning of 'obligation' (ijab) here is causation, i.e., that Allah's reward arises from His essence necessarily like the movement of a ring arising from the movement of the finger by way of causation. And He alluded by His statement 'without obligation' to the refutation of the philosophers. If you say: How is His statement 'without obligation' a refutation of the philosophers, given that the philosophers deny [reward] in its origin, which necessitates that they affirm no reward at all, neither by obligation nor otherwise? The answer to this: They deny the resurrection of bodies and affirm the resurrection of spirits. In their view, spirits are rewarded with spiritual pleasures and punished with spiritual pains and distresses. And He alluded by His statement 'and without necessity' (wujub) to the refutation of the Mu'tazila, who affirm the obligation of what is beneficial and most beneficial upon Allah. And the refutation of both groups will come, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (objection-and-answer) against two groups: philosophers and Mu'tazila. The commentator begins by explaining that 'obligation' (ijab) here means causal necessity, implying that reward does not issue from Allah by compulsion. He then presents the objection: if philosophers deny any reward at all, how can the statement 'without obligation' refute them? The answer: philosophers actually affirm spiritual reward and punishment, though they deny bodily resurrection. Thus, the refutation targets their flawed understanding of reward. Next, the commentator notes that the phrase 'without necessity' (wujub) refutes the Mu'tazila, who claim Allah must do what is best for creation. This is a standard Ash'ari rejection of moral obligation upon God. The answer is followed by a promise of future detailed refutation.

Key Terms
إيجاب
وجوب
الفلاسفة
المعتزلة
حشر الأجسام
حشر الأرواح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers deny any form of reward or punishment after death.
Correction: The passage clarifies that philosophers deny bodily resurrection but affirm spiritual reward and punishment, which the commentator still refutes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do philosophers view reward and punishment?
A) They deny any reward or punishment.
B) They affirm bodily reward and punishment.
C) They affirm spiritual reward and punishment while denying bodily resurrection.
D) They affirm only punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position firmly rejects any notion of obligation or necessity upon Allah, whether causal (philosophers) or moral (Mu'tazila).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's actions, do you ever subconsciously hold Him to a standard of what you think is 'fair' or 'just'? How does this passage's assertion that Allah is free from any obligation challenge that tendency?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that Allah must do what is most beneficial; the Ash'aris say He is free. Which view do you find yourself leaning toward in your heart? What would change if you fully internalized that Allah owes you nothing?
?
Do you sometimes feel entitled to reward from Allah because of your deeds? How does the statement that reward arises from His essence 'without obligation' reshape your understanding of His mercy and your relationship with Him?

Classification of Divine Attributes by Type of Evidence
Sanusi enumerates three categories of divine attributes based on evidence type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of divine attributes based on the type of evidence required to prove them: rational, scriptural, or both.

Core Idea
Not all divine attributes are proven the same way; some require reason, others scripture.

Before we can properly argue about God's attributes, we need to know which ones can be known by reason alone and which require revealed texts. The Ash'ari tradition classifies them into three categories, and this passage from Sanusi's commentary lays out that classification. It also clarifies a potential confusion about the phrase 'by reason' in the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'أصلا لا أصليا' and continues through the enumeration of the three categories. Then read the translation.
Notice the three categories are introduced with 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
2
Identify the three categories
Find each category: (1) attributes requiring rational proof only, (2) attributes requiring scriptural proof only, (3) the disputed attribute (unity). Understand which attributes fall into each group.
3
Analyze the commentator's remark on 'by reason'
The passage discusses why the commentator said 'by reason' (عقلا) for the obligatory attributes. Recognize the three interpretations: majority, weak, or ellipsis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصلا لا أصليا ولا فرعيا كما هو المنقول عن الأشاعرة وجمع من غيرهم، والمراد أن يعرف الواجب الله تعالى، وما عطف عليه، أعني قوله: كوجوده تعالى وقدمه وبقائه، وقيامه بنفسه ومخالفته للحوادث، وعلمه وقدرته وإرادته وحياته. الثاني: ما لا يصلح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالشرع، وهو كل ما لا يتوقف عليه دلالة المعجزة، وهي السمع والبصر والكلام، وكونه سميعا بصيرا متكلما. الثالث ما اختلف فيه وهو: الوحدانية. إذا عرفت ذلك تعرف أن قوله: (عقلا) بالنظر لغالب الصفات، أو المراد: عقلا ولو على وجه الضعف، أو أن في العبارة حذفا: أي سمعا، وقدر الشارح عقلا دفعا للإيهام، فالوجوب الأول بالشرع والثاني بالعقل، لكن الأولى أن يراد بالوجوب الثاني عدم الانفكاك مطلقا؛ لأن مباحث السمع والبصر والكلام المعول عليه فيها الدليل السمعي. وأما الصفات الباقية ولو الوحدانية لقولهم التعدد مؤد للعجز وعدم وجود شيء، فالتعويل فيها على العقلي لا السمعي، وإلا لتوقفت على السمع المتوقف على المعجزة، المتوقفة كسائر الأفعال على هذه الصفات فيدور. وبيان ذلك: أنه لو استدل على القدرة مثلا بالسمع فكانت القدرة متوقفة على السمع، والسمع عبارة عن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع، وهو متوقف على المعجزة، وهي متوقفة على قدرة الرب، فيلزم توقف القدرة على نفسها؛ لأن المتوقف على المتوقف متوقف، هكذا اشتهر، وفيه أن الجهة منفكة؛ إذ المعجزة تتوقف على وجود هذه الصفات لله تعالى خارجا لكونها لا توجد إلا بها، ولا تتوقف على معرفتها، ألا ترى أنها تقوم حجة على منكر وجاهل محض، والمتوقف على السمع والمعجزة معرفتها والحكم بها، أي: وجودها الذهني لا الخارجي، ولو صح هذا الدور للزم بالأولى في الدليل العقلي، فإنه بنفسه، والنظر فيه يتوقف على الصفات بلا واسطة شيء؛ إذ لم يخرج عن كونه فعلا من الأفعال، ومما لا يرد أيضا ما في شرح «الكبرى عن المقترح أن الاستدلال بالسمع على الكلام فيه دور، أي: استدلال على الشيء بنفسه، وأنت خبير بأن المدلول الصفة القائمة بالذات، والدليل من الكلام اللفظي.
Absolutely, neither essential nor subsidiary as is transmitted from the Ash'aris and a group of others. The intent is that one should know the Necessary [Being]—God Almighty—and what is conjoined with Him, namely His statement: such as His existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to events, and His knowledge, power, will, and life. Second: that which is not suitable to be used as evidence except by the Law, and that is everything on which the indication of the miracle does not depend, namely hearing, sight, and speech, and His being hearing, seeing, speaking. Third: that which is disputed, namely unity (al-waḥdāniyya). When you know that, you know that His statement 'by reason' is with consideration for the majority of attributes, or it means: by reason even if weakly, or that there is an ellipsis in the phrase, i.e., by hearing, and the commentator estimated 'by reason' to avoid misunderstanding. Thus the first obligation is by the Law and the second by reason, but it is better that by the second obligation is meant absolute inseparability, because the investigations of hearing, sight, and speech rely on scriptural evidence. As for the remaining attributes, even unity, because of their statement that multiplicity leads to incapacity and non-existence of something, reliance in them is on rational [evidence] not scriptural; otherwise they would depend on scripture which depends on the miracle, which depends like other acts on these attributes, leading to circularity. An explanation of that: if one were to use scripture as evidence for power, for example, then power would depend on scripture, and scripture is the Book, Sunnah, and consensus, and it depends on the miracle, and the miracle depends on the Lord's power, so the dependence of power on itself would follow; because what depends on a dependent is dependent. This is how it is known. But there is a distinction of aspects: the miracle depends on the existence of these attributes of God externally because they only exist through them, and it does not depend on knowledge of them. Do you not see that it is a proof against a denier and a pure ignorant? What depends on scripture and the miracle is their knowledge and judgment—i.e., their mental existence, not external. And if this circularity were valid, it would necessarily apply more to rational evidence, for it itself, and reflection on it depends on the attributes without any intermediary, since it is nothing but an act among acts. Also, what is mentioned in the commentary on al-Kubrā from al-Muqtaḥir that using scripture as evidence for speech involves circularity—i.e., using the thing itself as evidence for itself—does not apply, and you are well aware that the indicated thing is the attribute subsisting in the essence, and the evidence is verbal speech.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a classification of God's attributes based on the type of evidence required to prove them. It begins by implicitly stating three categories: (1) attributes that require rational proof alone—these are the attributes upon which the very possibility of miracles depends (e.g., existence, pre-eternity, power); (2) attributes that require scriptural proof alone—these are those upon which the indication of a miracle does not depend (e.g., God's hearing, sight, speech); and (3) the disputed attribute—unity (al-waḥdāniyya). The passage then explains the commentator's use of the term 'by reason' (ʿaqlan) in the matn: either it applies to most attributes, or it is used loosely, or there is an ellipsis meaning 'by hearing' but the commentator clarified it as reason to avoid confusion. It then argues that for the remaining attributes (including unity), rational evidence is primary; otherwise, if we relied on scripture, a circularity would occur: proving power via scripture would require scripture to be proven via miracle, which depends on power, leading to an infinite regress. The passage addresses a potential objection: the circularity is only in mental existence (knowledge) not external existence, but the argument from circularity is even stronger for rational proof because reasoning itself depends on the attributes. Finally, it refutes a specific objection regarding proving speech by scripture, noting that the evidence (verbal speech) is different from the attribute (speech subsisting in God's essence).

Key Terms
الصفات
الدليل العقلي
الدليل السمعي
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes can be proven by reason alone.
Correction: Some attributes, like God's hearing, sight, and speech, can only be proven by scripture because they do not affect the validity of miracles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following attributes requires scriptural proof?
Existence
Pre-eternity
Speech
Power

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are classified into those proven by reason, those proven by scripture, and a disputed one (unity), based on their relationship to miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that some of God's attributes can only be known through scripture. Are you as diligent in seeking knowledge from revelation as you are from reason? Which attribute—hearing, sight, or speech—do you most need to be reminded of today, and how will you actively remember it?
?
The attribute of unity (tawhid) is disputed in its evidence: some say reason proves it, others say only scripture. Does this ambiguity affect your certainty in tawhid? What does your heart rely on for that certainty?
?
If you were to only use reason to understand God, you would miss hearing, sight, and speech. What does that reveal about the limits of your own intellect? How will you humble yourself before revelation today?

The Circularity of Proving Attributes by Scripture
Sanusi argues that proving foundational attributes by scripture leads to circularity and distinguishes between mental and external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proving certain attributes (like power) by scripture leads to circular reasoning, and how the response distinguishes between external and mental existence.

Core Idea
Using scripture to prove foundational attributes creates a vicious circle; reason must come first.

The previous lesson established which attributes require rational proof. But why can't we just prove all attributes from revelation? This passage gives a powerful argument: if we tried to prove power by scripture, we would need scripture to be proven by a miracle, which depends on power, leading to a circle. Sanusi then addresses a potential objection about the nature of dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the circularity argument passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'إذا عرفت ذلك تعرف أن قوله ...' to the end. Follow the translation.
2
Trace the chain of dependence
Walk through the logical chain: if power is proven by scripture, then power depends on scripture. Scripture depends on the miracle. The miracle depends on power. So power depends on itself.
This is the core argument against relying on scripture for foundational attributes.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The passage notes a potential flaw: the circularity only applies to knowledge (mental existence) of the attributes, not their external existence. Sanusi argues that even so, the same circularity would apply to rational proof.
4
Understand the final clarification
The passage ends by refuting a specific objection about proving speech by scripture, noting that the evidence (verbal speech) is distinct from the attribute (speech in God's essence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

According to the passage, why can attributes like power not be proven solely by scripture?
Because scripture is not reliable for such attributes.
Because it would lead to a circular dependence: power depends on scripture, scripture depends on miracle, miracle depends on power.
Because the Ash'aris deny that scripture can prove any attribute.

?
When you recite the verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' (39:62), do you accept it simply because it is in the Quran, or have you reasoned to see why it must be true? ?

Have you ever unconsciously fallen into a similar circular reasoning in your own faith? For example, believing something because 'the Quran says so' while the Quran's authority itself depends on the very thing you are trying to prove? Write down one belief you hold on purely scriptural authority and ask yourself: 'Could I defend this rationally if asked by a skeptic?'
?

In your daily worship, do you focus more on God's attributes as concepts in your mind, or do you truly believe they are real and active in your life? How can you transform your intellectual understanding of, say, God's power into a lived trust that affects your decisions today? Commit to one specific action this week that demonstrates your reliance on God's power.

Divine Attributes

Three Divisions of Divine Attributes by Source of Proof
Classification of divine attributes by source of proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of divine attributes based on their source of proof: attributes known solely by reason, those known only by revelation, and the disputed attribute of oneness, as well as the nuanced interpretation of the phrase 'by reason' (عقلا) in the context of avoiding circularity.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are proven by reason, revelation, or a combination.

In this passage, the author lays out a foundational classification of divine attributes according to how they are known. Some attributes are self-evident to reason, others require scriptural evidence, and one attribute—oneness—is debated. The discussion also clarifies a potential ambiguity in the original text regarding the phrase 'by reason', ensuring we understand its intended meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and note the three categories the author lists. The first is attributes known by reason, the second those known by revelation, and the third the disputed one of oneness.
The author uses 'الثاني' and 'الثالث' to mark them.
2
Understand the first category
The first category includes attributes like existence, pre-eternity, perpetuity, etc. These are rationally necessary for God.
These are attributes that the intellect affirms without need for revelation.
3
Understand the second and third categories
The second category includes hearing, sight, and speech, known only through revelation. The third is oneness, which is disputed.
For the second category, the proof is solely revealed; for oneness, there is scholarly disagreement.
4
Analyze the discussion of 'عقلا'
The author offers several interpretations of the phrase 'by reason' to avoid circularity. Consider each interpretation and how it resolves the potential problem.
The preferred interpretation is that 'by reason' is understood broadly for attributes like oneness to include a rational element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي
أصلا (۱) ، لا أصليًّا ولا فرعيًّا كما هو المنقول عن الأشاعرة وجمع من غيرهم ، والمراد أن يعرف الواجب (۲) الله تعالى ، وما عُطف عليه ، أعني قوله:
||
کوجوده تعالى وقدمه وبقائه ، وقيامه بنفسه ومخالفته للحوادث، وعلمه وقدرته وإرادته وحياته. الثاني: : ما لا يصلح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالشرع ، وهو كل ما لا يتوقف عليه دلالة المعجزة، وهي السمع والبصر والكلام، وكونه سميعا بصيرا متكلما .
الثالث ما اختلف فيه وهو: الوحدانية .
إذا عرفت ذلك تعرف أن قوله : (عقلا) بالنظر لغالب الصفات، أو المراد: عقلا ولو على وجه الضعف ، أو أن في العبارة حذفا :أي: سمعا ، وقدر الشارح عقلا دفعا للإيطاء ، فالوجوب الأول بالشرع والثاني بالعقل، لكن الأولى أن يراد بالوجوب الثاني عدم الانفكاك مطلقا ؛ لأن مباحث السمع والبصر والكلام المعول عليه فيها الدليل السمعي. وأما الصفات الباقية ولو الوحدانية لقولهم التعدد مؤد للعجز وعدم وجود شيء، فالتعويل فيها على العقلي السمعي ، وإلا لتوقفت على السمع المتوقف على المعجزة ، المتوقفة كسائر الأفعال على هذه الصفات فيدور.
Explanation of the divisions of the rational judgment. Originally (1), not principal nor subsidiary as transmitted from the Ash‘arites and a group of others. The intended meaning is that one knows the obligatory (2) God Most High, and what is coordinated with it, namely His statement: such as His existence Most High, His pre-eternity, His perpetuity, His self-subsistence, His opposition to accidents, His knowledge, His power, His will, and His life. The second: what is not valid to infer except by revelation, and it is everything on which the indication of the miracle does not depend, and they are hearing, sight, speech, and His being hearing, seeing, speaking. The third is what is disputed, and it is oneness. If you know that, you know that his statement: (by reason) is with consideration of the majority of attributes, or the meaning is: by reason even if weakly, or that in the phrase there is ellipsis: i.e. by revelation, and the commentator estimated 'by reason' to avoid hiatus. Thus the first obligation is by revelation and the second by reason, but the best is that the second obligation intends absolute non-separation, because the investigations of hearing, sight, and speech rely on revealed evidence. As for the remaining attributes, even oneness, because of their saying multiplicity leads to incapacity and non-existence of something, reliance is on the rational-revealed, otherwise they would depend on revelation which depends on the miracle, which depends like other acts on these attributes, thus leading to circularity.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes according to the source of proof: (1) those known by reason alone (e.g., existence, pre-eternity, perpetuity, self-subsistence, opposition to accidents, knowledge, power, will, life); (2) those known only by revelation (hearing, sight, speech); (3) the disputed attribute of oneness (tawḥīd). The author then interprets the term 'ʿaqlan' in the original text, suggesting it may refer to a weak rational proof or be an ellipsis for 'samʿan' (by revelation). He emphasizes that for the first group, reason is sufficient, while for the second, only revelation suffices. For the remaining attributes including oneness, the proof is a combination of reason and revelation, otherwise circularity would result.

جائز
ممتنع
عقلي
سمعي
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'عقلا' always means strictly by pure reason without any revelation.
Correction: The author shows that 'عقلا' can include a weak rational proof or even imply revelation to avoid circularity.

Checkpoint

A) Existence
B) Hearing
C) Oneness (الوحدانية)
D) Power

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are proven either by reason, revelation, or a combination, with the attribute of oneness occupying a special disputed status.

?
The passage shows that relying solely on reason for some attributes leads to correct knowledge, while for others it leads to circularity. In your own life, where might you be tempted to trust reason alone in matters of faith? What would it cost you to submit to revelation instead?
?
The attribute of oneness required a blend of reason and revelation. Is your own understanding of tawḥīd equally balanced, or have you neglected one source? Commit to studying both the rational proofs and the revealed texts on God's oneness this week.

Refuting Circularity in Proving Attributes by Revelation
Author refutes circularity by distinguishing external and mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the charge of circularity in proving divine attributes through revelation by distinguishing between external and mental existence.

Core Idea
The charge of circularity collapses once we distinguish external from mental existence.

In this passage, the author tackles a sophisticated objection: that proving attributes like power through revelation leads to an infinite regress or circular dependence. The objection seems compelling at first glance. But the author reveals a critical distinction that dismantles it entirely. Let’s see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that power depends on revelation, revelation on miracles, and miracles on power, thus forming a circle. Read that chain carefully.
Notice that the objection assumes all dependence is of the same kind.
2
Identify the refutation
The author separates external existence (what miracles depend on) from mental existence (what revelation provides). This breaks the circle because the dependence is on different aspects.
External existence: attributes must exist for miracles to happen. Mental existence: revelation gives knowledge of attributes, not their external reality.
3
Consider the broader implication
The author adds that if the objection were valid, it would also apply to rational proofs, which depend on attributes just as much.
This shows the objection proves too much and therefore must be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا وبيان ذلك: أنه لو استدل على القدرة مثلا بالسمع فكانت القدرة متوقفة على السمع ، والسمع عبارة عن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع، وهو متوقف على المعجزة، وهي متوقفة على قدرة الرب، فيلزم توقف القدرة على نفسها ؛ لأن المتوقف على المتوقف متوقف ، هكذا اشتهر ، وفيه أن الجهة منفكة؛ إذ المعجزة تتوقف على وجود هذه الصفات الله تعالى خارجا لكونها لا توجد إلا بها، ولا تتوقف على معرفتها، ألا ترى أنها تقوم حجة على منكر وجاهل محض، والمتوقف على السمع والمعجزة معرفتها والحكم بها، أي: وجودها الذهني لا الخارجي، ولو صح هذا الدور للزم بالأولى في الدليل العقلي ، فإنه بنفسه ، والنظر فيه يتوقف على الصفات بلا واسطة شيء؛ إذ لم يخرج عن كونه فعلا من الأفعال ، ومما لا يرد أيضا ما في شرح «الكبرى عن المقترح أن الاستدلال بالسمع على الكلام فيه دور ، أي: استدلال : على الشيء بنفسه، وأنت خبير بأن المدلول الصفة القائمة بالذات، والدليل من الكلام اللفظي.
No, and the explanation of that: if one were to infer power, for example, by revelation, then power would be dependent on revelation, and revelation is the Book, the Sunna, and consensus, and it is dependent on the miracle, and the miracle is dependent on the power of the Lord, so the dependence of power on itself would follow, because what is dependent on something dependent is dependent. Thus it is well-known. But the response is that the aspect is separable: because the miracle depends on the existence of these attributes of God Most High externally, since they exist only through them, and it does not depend on knowledge of them. Do you not see that it stands as proof against a denier and a pure ignorant? What depends on revelation and the miracle is knowledge of them and judgment by them, i.e., their mental existence, not external. And if this circularity were valid, it would apply a fortiori to rational proof, because it itself and reflection on it depend on the attributes without any intermediary, since it is nothing but an act among acts. Also not valid is what is in the commentary of al-Kubrā from al-Muqtaḥ that inference by revelation concerning speech involves circularity, i.e., inferring a thing by itself, while you know that the signified is the attribute subsisting in the essence, and the evidence is from verbal speech.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection claims that proving an attribute like power by revelation leads to circularity: power depends on revelation, revelation depends on miracles, miracles depend on power. The refutation distinguishes between external existence (al-wujūd al-khārijī) and mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī): miracles depend on the external existence of attributes, while revelation gives knowledge of them (mental existence). Thus no circularity occurs. The author adds that if circularity applied, it would also affect rational proofs, which are equally dependent on attributes.

Key Terms

استدلال
سمعي
وجود خارجي
وجود ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proving attributes by revelation leads to an inescapable logical circularity.
Correction: The circularity is only apparent; revelation depends on attributes for external existence but provides knowledge (mental existence) of them, so the dependence is not on the same aspect.

Checkpoint
What key distinction resolves the alleged circularity in proving attributes by revelation?
A) The attributes are self-evident
B) The miracle depends on external existence, while revelation gives knowledge (mental existence)
C) Revelation is unnecessary for proof
D) The circle is unbreakable

Key Takeaway
The apparent circularity in proving attributes by revelation is resolved by recognizing that revelation provides mental knowledge, not external existence, thus breaking the cycle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah’s attributes from scripture, do you ever treat that knowledge as mere information rather than a reality that demands your life to change? What would it look like for you to move from mental knowledge to external submission?
?
This lesson shows that a miracle proves the existence of attributes without you needing to first know them. Has there been a moment in your life where you experienced Allah’s power or mercy without fully understanding it? Did you let that experience lead you to deeper knowledge, or did you ignore it?
?
The objection confuses knowledge with existence. In your own faith, are there times you confuse having correct beliefs about Allah with actually living in the reality of His sovereignty? What step can you take today to bridge that gap?

Classification of Divine Attributes by Evidence Type
Classifies divine attributes into three categories based on evidence type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified into three categories based on the type of evidence (rational or transmitted) required to prove them.

Core Idea
Divine attributes fall into three evidence classes: rational-only, transmitted-only, or both.

The study of divine attributes requires knowing which attributes can be known through reason alone, which require revelation, and which can be known through both. This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid presents a clear classification that is foundational for kalam methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice how the author defines 'nazar' as inference and then lists the three divisions.
The passage uses technical terms like 'dalil 'aqli' and 'dalil sam'i'.
2
Identify each division
Find the three categories: rational-only, transmitted-only, and permissible by both. Note the attributes listed under each and the reasoning given for why they fall into that division.
The first division depends on miracles; the second does not.
3
Consider the significance
Ask yourself why this classification matters for Islamic theology. How does it affect how we prove God's attributes?
This classification guides theologians in selecting appropriate proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالنظر: الاستدلال ، واعلم أن صفات الله تعالى بالنَّظر إلى الاستدلال عليها تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: القسم الأول: ما لا يصح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل العقلي ، وهو كل ما توقفت عليه المعجزة من صفاته سبحانه، كوجود تعالى وقدمه وبقائه وقيامه بنفسه ومخالفته للحوادث وقدرته وإرادته وعلمه وحياته . والقسم الثاني: ما لا يجوز الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل السمعي، وهو كل ما لا تتوقف عليه المعجزة من صفاته سبحانه ، كالسمع، والبصر، والكلام. والقسم الثالث: ما يجوز الاستدلال عليه بكلّ واحدٍ من الدليلين العقلي والسمعي، واختلف العلماء في الذي يثبت به ، وهو الوحدانية ، والأصح أن دليلها عقلي.
What is meant by 'nazar' is inference (istidlal). Know that the attributes of Allah, exalted, with respect to inference upon them, are divided into three divisions: The first division: that for which inference is not correct except by rational evidence (dalil 'aqli), and it is everything that the miracle depends upon of His attributes, exalted, such as existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, being unlike the originated, power, will, knowledge, and life. The second division: that for which inference is not permissible except by transmitted evidence (dalil sam'i), and it is everything that the miracle does not depend upon of His attributes, exalted, such as hearing, sight, and speech. The third division: that for which inference is permissible by each one of the two evidences, rational and transmitted, and the scholars differed regarding that by which it is established, and it is oneness (wahdaniyya), and the more correct is that its evidence is rational.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It explains that 'nazar' (reasoning) means istidlal (inference). It then divides God's attributes with respect to the type of evidence required into three categories. The first category includes attributes that can only be inferred by rational evidence (dalil 'aqli) – these are the attributes on which the validity of miracles depends, such as existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, being unlike the originated, power, will, knowledge, and life. The second category includes attributes that can only be inferred by transmitted evidence (dalil sam'i) – these are attributes on which miracles do not depend, such as hearing, sight, and speech. The third category includes attributes that can be inferred by either evidence, and this is oneness (tawhid); scholars differed on which evidence establishes it, but the correct view is that its evidence is rational. This classification shows the different epistemological bases for affirming God's attributes in kalam.

مخالفة للحوادث

حياة
سمع
بصر
كلام
وحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes require both rational and scriptural proof.
Correction: This passage shows that some attributes require only rational evidence, some only scriptural evidence, and some can be proven by either.

Checkpoint

A) Existence
B) Hearing
C) Oneness (wahdaniyya)
D) Power

Key Takeaway
The classification of attributes by evidence type shows that some attributes are known only through reason, some only through revelation, and some through both, guiding the theologian in choosing appropriate proofs.

Classification of Divine Attributes by Evidence Type
Classifies God's attributes according to evidence type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified according to the type of evidence (rational vs. scriptural) that proves them.

Core Idea
Not all divine attributes are known by reason alone; some require revelation.

This passage from al-Mureed's commentary on Jawharat al-Tawheed explains how scholars categorize God's attributes based on the evidence used to prove them. It distinguishes between attributes that can only be known through rational argument, those that can only be known through revealed texts, and one attribute that can be known by either. This classification is crucial for understanding Islamic theology's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'naẓar'
Notice that 'naẓar' is defined as 'istidlāl' (inference). This sets the context for the discussion of evidence types.
The term is used in the broader sense of rational inquiry.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage explicitly lists three categories of attributes based on whether they require rational evidence, scriptural evidence, or can be proven by either.
3
Examine the first division: rational evidence only
These are attributes upon which the validity of miracles depends, such as existence, pre-eternity, power, will, etc. Reason alone must prove them.
4
Examine the second division: scriptural evidence only
These attributes (hearing, sight, speech) do not depend on miracles and are known only through revelation.
5
Consider the third division: oneness (tawḥīd)
Scholars disagree whether oneness is proven by reason or scripture, but the soundest view is that it is rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) المراد بالنظر: الاستدلال ، واعلم أن صفات الله تعالى بالنَّظر إلى الاستدلال عليها تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: القسم الأول: ما لا يصح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل العقلي ، وهو كل ما توقفت عليه المعجزة من صفاته سبحانه، كوجود تعالى وقدمه وبقائه وقيامه بنفسه ومخالفته للحوادث وقدرته وإرادته وعلمه وحياته . والقسم الثاني: ما لا يجوز الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل السمعي، وهو كل ما لا تتوقف عليه المعجزة من صفاته سبحانه ، كالسمع، والبصر، والكلام. والقسم الثالث: ما يجوز الاستدلال عليه بكلّ واحدٍ من الدليلين العقلي والسمعي، واختلف العلماء في الذي يثبت به ، وهو الوحدانية ، والأصح أن دليلها عقلي. محي.
(1) The meaning of 'reasoning' (naẓar) is inference (istidlāl). Know that the attributes of God, Most High, in terms of inference concerning them are divided into three divisions: The first division: that which is not valid to infer except by rational evidence (dalīl ʿaqlī), and it is every attribute of His, exalted, upon which the miracle (muʿjiza) depends, such as His existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, being different from accidents, power, will, knowledge, and life. The second division: that which is not permissible to infer except by scriptural evidence (dalīl samʿī), and it is every attribute of His, exalted, upon which the miracle does not depend, such as hearing, sight, and speech. The third division: that which is valid to infer by each one of the two evidences, rational and scriptural. The scholars differed concerning which evidence proves it—and it is oneness (al-waḥdāniyya)—and the soundest opinion is that its evidence is rational. End.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on the term 'naẓar' (reasoning) used in the previous definition. It classifies divine attributes according to the type of evidence (dalīl) that proves them, producing a threefold division. First are attributes that can only be established by rational evidence (dalīl ʿaqlī); these are the attributes on which the validity of miracles depends—like existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, being unlike originated things, power, will, knowledge, and life. Reason alone must prove these, because the proof of prophethood through miracles presupposes a rational foundation about God. Second are attributes that can only be established by scriptural evidence (dalīl samʿī), namely hearing, sight, and speech; these are not necessary for establishing the miracle, so they are known only through revelation. Third is an attribute that can be proved by either type of evidence—oneness (tawḥīd). There is scholarly disagreement on which evidence is primary, but the strongest position is that it is rational.

Key Terms
النظر (al-naẓar)
الاستدلال (al-istidlāl)
الدليل العقلي (al-dalīl al-ʿaqlī)
الدليل السمعي (al-dalīl al-samʿī)
المعجزة (al-muʿjiza)
الوحدانية (al-waḥdāniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes are equally accessible to human reason.
Correction: This passage shows that some attributes (like hearing, sight, speech) can only be known through revelation, while others require reason.

Checkpoint

A) Existence and power
B) Hearing, sight, and speech
C) Oneness (al-waḥdāniyya)
D) Life and will

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are classified into three categories based on whether they are proven by rational evidence, scriptural evidence, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Self-Subsistent Attribute of Existence
Defines self-subsistent attribute of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is called a self-subsistent attribute and what it means for God to exist by His essence.

Core Idea
God's existence is essential, uncaused, and never ceases.

The commentator begins by dividing God's necessary attributes into four categories, the first of which is the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya). This lesson explains that existence (al-wujud) is such an attribute, meaning God exists by His own essence without any cause, and thus cannot be subject to non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the commentator defines existence as 'self-subsistent' and 'essential' (al-dhati).
The term 'self-subsistent' means the attribute is not dependent on anything else.
2
Understand the meaning of 'self-subsistent'
The commentator says existence means 'He existed by His essence, not by a cause'. This implies that God's existence is intrinsic and necessary, not contingent.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Because existence is by essence, it cannot accept non-existence, neither in the past nor the future. This establishes God's eternal and everlasting existence.
This contrasts with created beings, whose existence is caused and temporary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْقِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ: الصِّفَةُ النَّفْسِيَّةُ
[أَقْسَامُ الصِّفَاتِ الْوَاجِبَةِ لِلَّهِ تَعَالَى أَرْبَعَةٌ] [الْقِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ: الصِّفَةُ النَّفْسِيَّةُ]
[الْوُجُودُ]
٢٢ - فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ الْوُجُودُ وَالْقِدَمُ كَذَا بَقَاءٌ لَا يُشَابُ بِالْعَدَمِ
إِذَا أَرَدْتَ مَعْرِفَةَ مَا يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى (فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ) صِفَةٌ نَفْسِيَّةٌ (الْوُجُودُ) الذَّاتِيُّ، بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ وُجِدَ لِذَاتِهِ، لَا لِعِلَّةٍ؛ فَلَا يَقْبَلُ الْعَدَمَ، لَا أَزَلًا وَلَا أَبَدًا.
أَشَارَ الشَّارِحُ بِقَوْلِهِ: «إِذَا أَرَدْتَ مَعْرِفَةَ .. إِلَخْ» إِلَى أَنَّ الْفَاءَ فِي قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ» الْفَاءُ الَّتِي تُسَمَّى فَاءَ الْفَصِيحَةِ، وَهِيَ الَّتِي أَفْصَحَتْ عَنْ شَرْطٍ مُقَدَّرٍ. وَالضَّمِيرُ الْمَجْرُورُ مَحَلًّا بِاللَّامِ فِي قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ» عَائِدٌ عَلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى. وَ«وَاجِبٌ» فِي قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ» خَبَرٌ مُقَدَّمٌ، وَقَوْلُهُ: «الْوُجُودُ» مُبْتَدَأٌ مُؤَخَّرٌ، وَقَدَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ الْخَبَرَ لِلِاهْتِمَامِ بِهِ لَا لِإِفَادَةِ الْحَصْرِ عَلَى مَا قِيلَ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ هُنَا هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوُجُودِ لَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ. وَمِنَ النَّاسِ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّ قَوْلَهُ: «وَاجِبٌ» مُبْتَدَأٌ، وَسَوَّغَ الِابْتِدَاءَ بِهِ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ نَكِرَةً أَنَّهُ عَامِلٌ فِي الْجَارِّ وَالْمَجْرُورِ بَعْدَهُ، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَهُ: «لَهُ» جَارٌّ وَمَجْرُورٌ بِوَاجِبٍ، وَقَوْلُهُ: «الْوُجُودُ» خَبَرُ الْمُبْتَدَأِ. وَعَلَّلَ هَذَا بِأَنَّ الْأَصْلَ هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِالْمَجْهُولِ عَلَى الْمَعْلُومِ، وَالْمَعْلُومُ مِنْ قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ فِيمَا سَبَقَ ... إِلَخْ» هُوَ الْوُجُوبُ. وَقَدْ بَيَّنَهُ هُنَا بِقَوْلِهِ: "فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ الْوُجُودُ"، وَكَأَنَّهُ قَدْ قَالَ الْوَاجِبُ لَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ الَّذِي تَقَدَّمَتِ الْإِشَارَةُ إِلَيْهِ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ وَمَا عُطِفَ عَلَيْهِ. وَهَذَا الْوَجْهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ صَحِيحًا مِنْ حَيْثُ الْمَعْنَى، لَا يَتَّفِقُ مَعَ مَا ذَكَرَ النُّحَاةُ مِنْ لَمْ يُسْمَعْ عَنِ الْعَرَبِ الْإِخْبَارُ بِالْمَعْرِفَةِ عَنِ النَّكِرَةِ وَلَوْ مُخَصَّصَةً، لِهَذَا كَانَ الْأَوْلَى هُوَ الْوَجْهُ الْأَوَّلُ وَهُوَ جَعْلُ قَوْلِهِ: "فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ" خَبَرًا مُقَدَّمًا فَاعْرِفْ ذَلِكَ.
(٢) قَوْلُهُ: (فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ) أَيْ: اللَّهِ الْمُتَقَدِّمِ ذِكْرُهُ فِي قَوْلِهِ: "مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا اللَّهُ ... إِلَخْ"، وَالْوُجُودُ إِمَّا فَاعِلُ...
The first section: the self-subsistent attribute. [The divisions of the attributes obligatory for Allah Most High are four] [The first section: the self-subsistent attribute] [Existence] 22 - So wajib for Him is existence, and pre-eternity, likewise permanence unmingled with non-existence. When you wish to know what is wajib for Him Most High, (so wajib for Him) is a self-subsistent attribute, (existence) the essential, meaning that He existed by His essence, not by a cause; thus it does not accept non-existence, neither from eternity nor forever. The commentator indicated by his saying: 'When you wish to know... etc.' that the 'fa' in the author's saying 'fa-wajib lahu' is the 'fa' called 'fa' al-fasiha' (the fa of explicitness), which reveals an implied condition. The pronoun in the genitive case by the lam in the author's saying 'fa-wajib lahu' refers back to Allah Most High. And 'wajib' in the author's saying 'fa-wajib lahu' is a fronted predicate, and his saying 'al-wujud' is a delayed subject, and the author fronted the predicate for emphasis, not for exclusivity according to what was said, because the intended meaning here is the judgment of the necessity of existence for Him, glorified is He. Some people said that his saying 'wajib' is a subject, and the permissibility of beginning with it despite being indefinite is that it governs the prepositional phrase after it, for his saying 'lahu' is a prepositional phrase governed by 'wajib', and his saying 'al-wujud' is the predicate of the subject. They justified this by saying that the principle is to judge the unknown by the known, and the known from the author's saying previously: 'So everyone who is legally obligated must know what has become obligatory: Allah... etc.' is the obligation. And he explained it here by his saying: 'fa-wajib lahu al-wujud', as if he said: 'The wajib for Him, glorified is He, which was previously indicated, is existence and what is conjoined with it.' This view, although correct in meaning, does not agree with what the grammarians mentioned: that it has not been transmitted from the Arabs that they predicate a definite noun from an indefinite one, even if specified. Therefore, the first view is preferable, which is making his saying 'fa-wajib lahu' a fronted predicate. So know that. (2) His saying: (fa-wajib lahu) meaning: Allah, who was mentioned previously in his saying: 'what has become obligatory: Allah... etc.', and existence is either the subject...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage combines a doctrinal definition with a grammatical analysis of the poet's line. It begins by classifying the first of the four obligatory attributes of God: the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya), which is existence (al-wujud). Existence is described as 'essential' (al-dhati), meaning that God exists by His own essence, not due to any cause; consequently, He never accepts non-existence, neither in pre-eternity nor in the future. The commentator then explains the grammatical structure of the line, specifically the function of the particle 'fa' (called 'fa' al-fasiha') which implies a conditional clause. He clarifies that 'wajib lahu' is a fronted predicate (khabar muqaddam) and 'al-wujud' is a delayed subject (mubtada' mu'akhkhar), and the fronting is for emphasis, not for restriction. Then he presents an alternative grammatical analysis by some scholars who consider 'wajib' as the subject and 'lahu' as a prepositional phrase attached to it, and 'al-wujud' as the predicate. However, he criticizes this view because it violates an Arabic grammatical rule: a definite noun cannot be predicated from an indefinite noun. Therefore, the first analysis is preferable. The passage ends with a footnote reiterating the referent of the pronoun. Thus, the commentator ensures both theological accuracy and grammatical correctness.

Key Terms
الصفة النفسية
الوجود الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's existence is like created existence—caused and temporary.
Correction: The text emphasizes that God's existence is by His essence, not caused, and does not accept non-existence.

Checkpoint
What does it mean that existence is a 'self-subsistent attribute' (sifa nafsiyya)?
A) It depends on a cause and can cease to exist.
B) It is eternal in the past but can cease in the future.
C) It exists by itself, without cause, and does not accept non-existence.
D) It is an acquired attribute that God chose to have.

Key Takeaway
Existence is essential to God, uncaused, and forever free from non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you live as if your existence is a given, or do you recognize that every moment you exist is a direct act of Allah's sustaining? How would your daily mindset change if you truly internalized that without His continuous will, you would cease to exist?
?
The passage emphasizes that Allah's existence is essential and never ceases. In contrast, everything you love and rely on in this world is temporary. Are you investing your heart in things that will perish, or are you anchoring your hope in the One whose existence is permanent and uncaused? What is one attachment you need to loosen today because it will not last?
?
Knowing that Allah exists by His essence and not by any cause means that He is the ultimate reality from which all else derives. Does your heart truly submit to this, or do you find yourself acting as if the universe runs on its own? Commit to one specific action this week that consciously reminds you that Allah is the only self-subsistent Being.

Divine Attributes

Grammatical Analysis of 'Wajib Lahu al-Wujud'
Grammatical analysis of the phrase and debate on predicate/subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the phrase 'fa-wajib lahu al-wujud' and the debate over whether it involves a fronted predicate or an indefinite subject.

Core Idea
Grammar clarifies theology: the fronted predicate emphasizes God's existence.

The commentator now analyzes the grammar of the author's line, specifically the function of the particle 'fa' (fa' al-fasiha) and the order of words. He explains why 'wajib' is best considered a fronted predicate (khabar muqaddam) and refutes an alternative view that treats it as an indefinite subject. This lesson focuses on the grammatical reasoning that supports the intended theological emphasis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'fa' and its function
The commentator explains that the 'fa' in 'fa-wajib lahu' is 'fa' al-fasiha, which reveals an implied condition (e.g., 'if you want to know... then...'). This sets up the grammatical analysis.
The 'fa' links the statement to an implied conditional, making the sentence rhetorical.
2
Analyze the first grammatical view
The commentator states the first and correct view: 'wajib' is a fronted predicate (khabar muqaddam), and 'al-wujud' is a delayed subject (mubtada' mu'akhkhar). The fronting is for emphasis (ihtimam), not for exclusivity.
This view maintains the standard Arabic order of subject-predicate but emphasizes the predicate.
3
Examine the alternative view
Some scholars considered 'wajib' to be an indefinite subject (mubtada') with the prepositional phrase 'lahu' attached to it, and 'al-wujud' as the predicate. They justified it by saying the unknown (obligation) is judged by the known (existence).
This view violates a grammatical rule: a definite noun cannot be predicated from an indefinite.
4
Understand the refutation
The commentator rejects the alternative view because it contradicts the practice of the Arabs: it is not transmitted that they predicate a definite from an indefinite. Therefore, the first view (fronted predicate) is preferable.
The lesson concludes that the first view is both grammatically sound and theologically appropriate.

Source Text

الْقِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ: الصِّفَةُ النَّفْسِيَّةُ
[أَقْسَامُ الصِّفَاتِ الْوَاجِبَةِ لِلَّهِ تَعَالَى أَرْبَعَةٌ] [الْقِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ: الصِّفَةُ النَّفْسِيَّةُ]
[الْوُجُودُ]
٢٢ - فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ الْوُجُودُ وَالْقِدَمُ كَذَا بَقَاءٌ لَا يُشَابُ بِالْعَدَمِ
إِذَا أَرَدْتَ مَعْرِفَةَ مَا يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى (فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ) صِفَةٌ نَفْسِيَّةٌ (الْوُجُودُ) الذَّاتِيُّ، بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ وُجِدَ لِذَاتِهِ، لَا لِعِلَّةٍ؛ فَلَا يَقْبَلُ الْعَدَمَ، لَا أَزَلًا وَلَا أَبَدًا.
أَشَارَ الشَّارِحُ بِقَوْلِهِ: «إِذَا أَرَدْتَ مَعْرِفَةَ .. إِلَخْ» إِلَى أَنَّ الْفَاءَ فِي قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ» الْفَاءُ الَّتِي تُسَمَّى فَاءَ الْفَصِيحَةِ، وَهِيَ الَّتِي أَفْصَحَتْ عَنْ شَرْطٍ مُقَدَّرٍ. وَالضَّمِيرُ الْمَجْرُورُ مَحَلًّا بِاللَّامِ فِي قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ» عَائِدٌ عَلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى. وَ«وَاجِبٌ» فِي قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ: «فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ» خَبَرٌ مُقَدَّمٌ، وَقَوْلُهُ: «الْوُجُودُ» مُبْتَدَأٌ مُؤَخَّرٌ، وَقَدَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ الْخَبَرَ لِلِاهْتِمَامِ بِهِ لَا لِإِفَادَةِ الْحَصْرِ عَلَى مَا قِيلَ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ هُنَا هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِوُجُوبِ الْوُجُودِ لَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ. وَمِنَ النَّاسِ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّ قَوْلَهُ: «وَاجِبٌ» مُبْتَدَأٌ، وَسَوَّغَ الِابْتِدَاءَ بِهِ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ نَكِرَةً أَنَّهُ عَامِلٌ فِي الْجَارِّ وَالْمَجْرُورِ بَعْدَهُ، فَإِنَّ قَوْلَهُ: «لَهُ» جَارٌّ وَمَجْرُورٌ بِوَاجِبٍ، وَقَوْلُهُ: «الْوُجُودُ» خَبَرُ الْمُبْتَدَأِ. وَعَلَّلَ هَذَا بِأَنَّ الْأَصْلَ هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِالْمَجْهُولِ عَلَى الْمَعْلُومِ، وَالْمَعْلُومُ مِنْ قَوْلِ الْمُصَنِّفِ فِيمَا سَبَقَ ... إِلَخْ» هُوَ الْوُجُوبُ. وَقَدْ بَيَّنَهُ هُنَا بِقَوْلِهِ: "فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ الْوُجُودُ"، وَكَأَنَّهُ قَدْ قَالَ الْوَاجِبُ لَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ الَّذِي تَقَدَّمَتِ الْإِشَارَةُ إِلَيْهِ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ وَمَا عُطِفَ عَلَيْهِ. وَهَذَا الْوَجْهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ صَحِيحًا مِنْ حَيْثُ الْمَعْنَى، لَا يَتَّفِقُ مَعَ مَا ذَكَرَ النُّحَاةُ مِنْ لَمْ يُسْمَعْ عَنِ الْعَرَبِ الْإِخْبَارُ بِالْمَعْرِفَةِ عَنِ النَّكِرَةِ وَلَوْ مُخَصَّصَةً، لِهَذَا كَانَ الْأَوْلَى هُوَ الْوَجْهُ الْأَوَّلُ وَهُوَ جَعْلُ قَوْلِهِ: "فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ" خَبَرًا مُقَدَّمًا فَاعْرِفْ ذَلِكَ.
(٢) قَوْلُهُ: (فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ) أَيْ: اللَّهِ الْمُتَقَدِّمِ ذِكْرُهُ فِي قَوْلِهِ: "مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا اللَّهُ ... إِلَخْ"، وَالْوُجُودُ إِمَّا فَاعِلُ...
The first section: the self-subsistent attribute. [The divisions of the attributes obligatory for Allah Most High are four] [The first section: the self-subsistent attribute] [Existence] 22 - So wajib for Him is existence, and pre-eternity, likewise permanence unmingled with non-existence. When you wish to know what is wajib for Him Most High, (so wajib for Him) is a self-subsistent attribute, (existence) the essential, meaning that He existed by His essence, not by a cause; thus it does not accept non-existence, neither from eternity nor forever. The commentator indicated by his saying: 'When you wish to know... etc.' that the 'fa' in the author's saying 'fa-wajib lahu' is the 'fa' called 'fa' al-fasiha' (the fa of explicitness), which reveals an implied condition. The pronoun in the genitive case by the lam in the author's saying 'fa-wajib lahu' refers back to Allah Most High. And 'wajib' in the author's saying 'fa-wajib lahu' is a fronted predicate, and his saying 'al-wujud' is a delayed subject, and the author fronted the predicate for emphasis, not for exclusivity according to what was said, because the intended meaning here is the judgment of the necessity of existence for Him, glorified is He. Some people said that his saying 'wajib' is a subject, and the permissibility of beginning with it despite being indefinite is that it governs the prepositional phrase after it, for his saying 'lahu' is a prepositional phrase governed by 'wajib', and his saying 'al-wujud' is the predicate of the subject. They justified this by saying that the principle is to judge the unknown by the known, and the known from the author's saying previously: 'So everyone who is legally obligated must know what has become obligatory: Allah... etc.' is the obligation. And he explained it here by his saying: 'fa-wajib lahu al-wujud', as if he said: 'The wajib for Him, glorified is He, which was previously indicated, is existence and what is conjoined with it.' This view, although correct in meaning, does not agree with what the grammarians mentioned: that it has not been transmitted from the Arabs that they predicate a definite noun from an indefinite one, even if specified. Therefore, the first view is preferable, which is making his saying 'fa-wajib lahu' a fronted predicate. So know that. (2) His saying: (fa-wajib lahu) meaning: Allah, who was mentioned previously in his saying: 'what has become obligatory: Allah... etc.', and existence is either the subject...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage combines a doctrinal definition with a grammatical analysis of the poet's line. It begins by classifying the first of the four obligatory attributes of God: the self-subsistent attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya), which is existence (al-wujud). Existence is described as 'essential' (al-dhati), meaning that God exists by His own essence, not due to any cause; consequently, He never accepts non-existence, neither in pre-eternity nor in the future. The commentator then explains the grammatical structure of the line, specifically the function of the particle 'fa' (called 'fa' al-fasiha') which implies a conditional clause. He clarifies that 'wajib lahu' is a fronted predicate (khabar muqaddam) and 'al-wujud' is a delayed subject (mubtada' mu'akhkhar), and the fronting is for emphasis, not for restriction. Then he presents an alternative grammatical analysis by some scholars who consider 'wajib' as the subject and 'lahu' as a prepositional phrase attached to it, and 'al-wujud' as the predicate. However, he criticizes this view because it violates an Arabic grammatical rule: a definite noun cannot be predicated from an indefinite noun. Therefore, the first analysis is preferable. The passage ends with a footnote reiterating the referent of the pronoun. Thus, the commentator ensures both theological accuracy and grammatical correctness.

Key Terms
فاء الفصيحة
خبر مقدم
مبتدأ مؤخر

معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the fronting in 'wajib lahu al-wujud' implies exclusivity (that only existence is obligatory).
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is for emphasis (ihtimam), not exclusivity, as other attributes follow.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer the view that 'wajib' is a fronted predicate rather than an indefinite subject?
A) Because the alternative violates the rule that a definite cannot be predicated from an indefinite.
B) Because the alternative does not make sense theologically.
C) Because the 'fa' is a conditional particle.
D) Because the author explicitly said so.

Key Takeaway
The correct grammatical analysis of 'wajib lahu al-wujud' treats 'wajib' as a fronted predicate for emphasis, avoiding a violation of Arabic grammar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might overlooking grammar lead to a flawed belief about Allah? Commit to learning one grammatical principle from this lesson and applying it when you encounter similar phrases.
?
The commentator chose the first analysis because it avoids violating a rule of Arabic, even though the second is correct in meaning. Are you willing to abandon an interpretation that seems correct if it conflicts with established linguistic norms? Examine your own approach to understanding revelation: do you prioritize meaning over method, or vice versa? What would it take for you to change your mind?
?
The passage ends with 'fa'rif dhālika' (know that). How does this call to certainty challenge you? Do you seek certainty in your theological knowledge, or are you content with ambiguity? Reflect on one area of your faith where you have settled for unclear understanding and resolve to pursue clarity through study.

Existence as a Self-Attribute and Its Proof
Sanusi defines existence as a self-attribute and proves its necessity by world dependence and rejection of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is considered a self-attribute of Allah and how the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress prove its necessity.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is essential, not caused, and the world's dependence demands it be necessary.

Sanusi begins the discussion of divine attributes with existence, establishing it as a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya). The proof relies on the world's dependence on Allah and the absurdity of circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) if His existence were merely possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three attributes mentioned at the start and then the focused explanation on existence.
Notice the matn line is followed by commentary.
2
Understand 'self-attribute'
The passage defines existence as a self-attribute, meaning it is essential and not derived from a cause. Reflect on why this distinguishes Allah from contingent beings.
This concept is central to kalam.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof states: the world and every part of it depends on Allah. Therefore, Allah's existence must be necessary, not possible. If possible, it would require a cause, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
These two fallacies are considered impossible.
4
Connect to rational judgments
Identify that necessity (wujub) is the first rational judgment applied to Allah. This sets the foundation for other attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٢ - فَواجِبُ لـه الـوجـود والقِدَمُ كَذَا بَقَاءُ لا يُشَابُ بالعَدَمُ إذا أردت معرفة ما يجب له تعالى (فَوَاجِبٌ لَهُ) صفةٌ نفسيَّةٌ (الوُجُودُ) الذَّاتِيُّ ، بمعنى أنَّه وُجد لذاته ، لا لعلَّةٍ ؛ فلا يقبل العدم، لا أزلا ولا أبدا ؛ لوجوب افتقار العالم وكلّ جزء من أجزائه إليه تعالى وكُلٌّ مَن وجب افتقارُ العالم إليه لا يكونُ وجوده إلَّا واجبا ، لا جائزاً ؛ وإلَّا لزمَ الدَّورُ أو التّسلسلُ .
22 - So necessary for Him are existence, pre-eternity, and likewise permanence not mixed with non-existence. If you wish to know what is necessary for Him, then necessary for Him is a self-attribute: existence, the essential, meaning that He exists by His essence, not by a cause; thus He does not admit non-existence, neither in pre-eternity nor post-eternity; due to the necessity of the world's dependence and every part of it on Him, and whoever the world's dependence is necessary upon, His existence cannot be but necessary, not possible; otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines the first division of necessary attributes: the self-attribute of existence. It begins with the matn line stating that necessary for Allah are existence, pre-eternity, and permanence. The commentary then focuses on existence, explaining that it is a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya), meaning essential existence (wujud dhati), i.e., He exists by His essence, not through a cause. Consequently, He does not admit non-existence in pre-eternity or post-eternity. The proof is given: the entire world and every part of it is necessarily dependent upon Allah; therefore, the one upon whom the world depends cannot have merely possible existence—His existence must be necessary. If it were possible, it would require a cause, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both absurd. Thus, existence is a necessary attribute of Allah, demonstrating the first rational judgment: necessity (wujub).

وجوب (wujub)
جواز (jawaz)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Correction: The passage clarifies existence is a self-attribute (sifa nafsiyya), meaning it is identical with the essence, not a separate quality.

Checkpoint

A) Because He is eternal and has no beginning.
B) Because the world is dependent on Him, and possible existence would lead to circularity or infinite regress.
C) Because He has no cause for His existence.
D) Because His existence is same as His essence.

Key Takeaway
Allah's existence is a self-attribute (essential) and must be necessary because the world depends on Him; otherwise, circularity or infinite regress would result.

?
Sanusi argues that if Allah's existence were merely possible, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity. This means the very structure of reality demands a Necessary Being. Does your daily life reflect this dependence on the Necessary? Do you live as if you are contingent, moment by moment, or do you slip into a feeling of self-sufficiency? Choose one small action today—like drinking water—and reflect that even that is sustained by the One whose existence is necessary.

Do you ever stop to realize that your next heartbeat is only possible because He exists necessarily? Pause now and ask yourself: Am I living with the awe and reverence due to the Self-Existing?
